Inheritance of a Fetus: Suspension of Estate Distribution
Ruling and proof on holding estate for an unborn child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on suspending estate distribution when a fetus is present and identify the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
The fetus is a potential heir; its share must be reserved until birth, assuming male for precaution.

When a man dies leaving a pregnant wife, the unborn child (fetus) is a potential heir. To ensure fair distribution, the estate is suspended until the child is born. If there are other children, the fetus's share is set aside assuming it is a male (since a male's share is larger) to avoid the difficulty of recovering distributed shares if a male is born. This ruling is based on a hadith that a newborn who cries inherits, implying the fetus is a legal person once born alive. Let us examine the ruling and its proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the distribution of the estate of a deceased who leaves a pregnant wife. The ruling: the estate is suspended (mawquf) until the child is born. If there are no other children, full suspension; if there are other children, a share is set aside for the fetus assuming it is a male.
This is a procedural ruling to protect the potential heir's right.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is a hadith: 'When a newborn cries, he inherits.' This is reported by Abu Hurairah in Abu Dawud and Ibn Majah.
The hadith is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof supports the ruling)
The hadith directly links crying (a sign of life) to inheritance. If a child is born alive, it inherits. Therefore, the fetus must be considered a potential heir from the moment of death, and its share must be preserved until birth to ensure it receives its right.
The ruling assumes the fetus will be born alive; the estate is held for that contingency.
4
Understand the practical precaution
If other children exist, the fetus's share is set aside assuming it is a male (son) because a son's share is larger than a daughter's. This avoids the problem of having to reclaim already distributed shares if a son is born. If a daughter is born, the excess is redistributed.
This is a precautionary measure based on Hanafi fiqh to prevent hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳۲۳۸) (۱۰) کسی کا انتقال ہوا اور حمل چھوڑا تو اس کا مال موقوف رہے گا۔ یہاں تک کہ اس کی بیوی حمل جن دے امام ابو حنیفہ کے قول میں ۔ ایک آدمی کا انتقال ہوا اس وقت اس کی بیوی حاملہ تھی ۔ تو اس حمل کو بھی باپ کی وراثت ملے گی۔ اس لئے اگر یہی ایک بچہ ہے تو ابھی وراثت تقسیم نہیں کی جائے گی ۔ بلکہ وضع حمل کے بعد وراثت تقسیم ہوگی۔ اور اگر دوسری اولاد بھی موجود ہے تو اس حمل کولڑ کا مان کر اس کا حصہ الگ رکھ لیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ لڑکے کا حصہ زیادہ ہوتا ہے اور لڑکی کا کم ۔ پس اگر لڑ کی کا حصہ مان کر باقی مال تقسیم کر دیا جائے اور لڑکا پیدا ہو جائے تو دوبارہ وارثین سے مال واپس کرنا مشکل ہے۔ اس لئے پہلے ہی احتیاط کر کے لڑکے کا حصہ رکھا جائے۔ تا کہ وارثین سے مال واپس نہ لینا پڑے۔ اور اگر لڑ کی پیدا ہوئی تو اس کو حصہ دینے کے بعد جو بچے گا وہ باقی وارثین کو بعد میں دے دیا جائے گا۔ حمل وارث ہو گا اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث گزر چکی ہے۔ عن ابي هرير عن النبی الله قال اذا استهل المولود ورث (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب فی المولود یستھل ثم یموت، ص ۴۹، نمبر ۲۹۲۰ رابن ماجہ شریف ، باب ما جاء فی الصلوة علی التطفل جس ۲۱۵ نمبر ۱۵۰۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ بچہ زندہ پیدا ہوتو وہ وارث ہوگا۔ اس سے یہ بھی ثابت ہوا کہ حمل ٹھہرنے کے بعد کوئی مرجائے تو اس کی وراثت حمل کو ملے گی اور اس کے لئے الگ کر کے مال رکھا جائیگا۔
(3238) (10) Whoever dies and leaves a pregnancy, his wealth will be suspended until his wife gives birth, according to Imam Abu Hanifa. If a man dies and his wife is pregnant, the fetus will also inherit from the father. Therefore, if there is only this child, the inheritance will not be distributed immediately; rather, after birth, the inheritance will be distributed. And if there are other children, the fetus is assumed to be a male and its share is set aside, because the son's share is greater and the daughter's share is less. So if one assumes it is a daughter and distributes the rest, and a son is born, it is difficult to take back wealth from the heirs. Therefore, precaution is taken by setting aside the son's share, so that wealth does not have to be returned from the heirs. And if a daughter is born, after giving her share, the remainder will be given later to the other heirs. The fetus inherits; its proof is the hadith that has passed: Abu Hurairah from the Prophet said: When a newborn cries, he inherits. (a) (Abu Dawud, Book of Newborn Crying then Dying, p. 49, hadith 2920; Ibn Majah, Book of Prayer over the Child, hadith 1508) From this hadith it is understood that if a child is born alive, he inherits. And it is also proven that when someone dies and leaves a pregnancy, the inheritance goes to the fetus and wealth is set aside for it.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the inheritance of a fetus (haml). The ruling states that if a man dies leaving his wife pregnant, the distribution of the estate is suspended until the child is born. This is based on the principle that the fetus is a potential heir. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the fetus does inherit. If the deceased has no other children, the entire estate is held until birth. If there are other children, the fetus is allocated a share assuming it is a male (since a male's share is larger) to be on the safe side. If a female is born, the excess is redistributed. The proof is a hadith: if a newborn cries (i.e., shows signs of life), it inherits. This indicates that the fetus is considered a living person for inheritance purposes once born alive. Thus, the estate is held until the child's birth to ensure proper distribution. The commentary provides practical reasoning to avoid the difficulty of reclaiming distributed shares.

Key Terms
حمل (haml): A fetus; an unborn child that may inherit if born alive.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; the estate is held back from distribution until a condition is met (birth of the child).
استهل (istahalla): The newborn cries; a sign of life that makes inheritance valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a fetus allocated a share assuming it is male in the Hanafi ruling?
A) Because males are always favored
B) To avoid having to take back distributed wealth if a male is born
C) Because the fetus is always male
D) There is no specific reason

Key Takeaway
The estate of a deceased with a pregnant wife is suspended until birth; the fetus inherits if born alive, and a precautionary male share is set aside.

Grandfather's Inheritance Precedence over Siblings
Intra-Hanafi dispute on grandfather vs siblings inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on grandfather's inheritance when siblings are present and explain the proofs for each view, identifying the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Grandfather excludes siblings according to Abu Hanifa, but shares with them according to the Sahibayn—a key intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

In this passage from Bāb al-Radd, the author addresses a significant intra-Hanafi dispute: does the paternal grandfather (jadd) exclude siblings from inheritance, or does he share with them? Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for correctly applying Hanafi inheritance law, as the mufta bihi position affects real estate distribution. The passage also briefly touches on the inheritance of a fetus as a preliminary precautionary measure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa holds that the grandfather is like a father and excludes siblings. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the grandfather shares equally with siblings, but never gets less than one-third.
Both views are recorded within Hanafi school.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa uses: (1) Athar from Abu Bakr, Ibn Abbas, and Ibn al-Zubayr that 'grandfather is a father', citing Quran where Ya'qub is called father though a grandfather. (2) Hadith of Imran ibn Husayn where the Prophet gave the grandfather two sixths, one as share and one as asabah, showing precedence over siblings.
The Quranic evidence equates grandfather with father in inheritance.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn rely on athar from Umar and Uthman who made the grandfather share with siblings: half with one brother, one-third with two, never less than one-third.
This practice of the two caliphs is their main evidence.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The author favors Abu Hanifa's proofs as stronger, noting that the hadith of Imran explicitly gives the grandfather an extra share as asabah, indicating he has superiority over siblings. The athar from the caliphs is contradicted by the explicit hadith.
The mufta bihi position is Abu Hanifa's view.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, siblings receive nothing if grandfather is present. Under the Sahibayn, siblings share the residue with grandfather. This practical difference is crucial in estate distribution.
For example: if estate = 12, only grandfather and one brother, Abu Hanifa gives 12 to grandfather, Sahibayn give 6 each.

امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک دادا بھائیوں سے مقدم ہے اس لئے پہلے ان کو دیا جائے گا۔ اس سے بچے گا تب بھائیوں کو دیا جائے گا۔ اور صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ اصحاب سہام کے بعد جو مال بچا اس میں دونوں کو آدھا آدھا دیا جائے۔ البتہ اگر آدھا آدھا دینے میں دادا کو تہائی سے کم ملے تو دادا کو پہلے تہائی دی جائے گی پھر جو بچے گا وہ بھائی کو دیا جائے گا۔

امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ اثر ہے۔ وقال ابو بكر وابن عباس وابن الزبير الجد اب وقرأ ابن عباس یا بنی آدم ( آیت ۲۶ ، سورۃ الاعراف)، اتبعت ملة آباءى ابراهيم واسحاق و یعقوب (ب) (آیت ۳۸ سوره یوسف ۱۲) ( بخاری شریف، باب میراث الجد مع الاب والاخوۃ ص ۹۹۷ نمبر ۶۷۳۷) اس اثر میں ہے کہ دادا کو باپ قرار دیا کیونکہ آیت میں بھی حضرت یعقوب کو باپ کہا حالانکہ وہ دادا ہیں۔ جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ دادا باپ کے درجے میں ہیں۔ اور باپ سے بھائی ساقط ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے دادا سے بھی بھائی ساقط ہوگا (۲) حدیث میں دادا کی اہمیت ہے۔ عن عمران بن حصین ان رجلا اتی النبی صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال ان ابن ابنی مات فمالی من ميراثه ؟ قال لك السدس فلما ادبر دعاه فقال لك سدس آخر فلما ادبر دعاه فقال ان السدس الآخر طعمة (ج) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب ما جاء فی میراث الجد ،ص ۴۵، نمبر ۲۸۹۶) اس حدیث میں دادا کو حصہ بھی دیا اور عصبہ کے طور پر بھی چھٹا حصہ دیا اس لئے بھائی کے مقابلے میں دادا کی اہمیت ہے۔

صاحبین کی دلیل یہ اثر ہے۔ عن الزهري قال كان عمر بن الخطاب يشرك بين الجد والاخ اذا لم يكن غيرهما ويجعل له الثلث مع الاخوين وما كانت المقاسمة خير ! له قاسم ولا ينفص من السدس في جميع المال (الف) دوسری روایت میں ہے۔ وحضرت الخليفتين قبلک ، يريد عمر و عثمان يقضيان للجد مع الاخ الواحد النصف ومع الاثنين الثلث فاذا كانوا أكثر من ذلك لم ينقص من الثلث شيئا (ب) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب فرض الجد ، ج عاشر ص ۲۶۶، نمبر ۱ ۱۹۰۶ر ۱۹۰۶۲) مصنف ابن ابی شیبیته ۴۴۰ از اترک اخوة و جدا واختلافهم فیه، ج سادس ،ص ۲۶۲ نمبر ۳۱۲۱۵) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ دادا کو بھائی کا آدھا تقسیم کر کے دیا جائے گا۔ البتہ تقسیم کرنے میں تہائی سے کم ہو تو دادا کو تہائی دی جائے گی۔ کیونکہ دوسرے اثر میں ہے لــم يـنـقـص مـن الثلث شینا کہ تہائی سے کم نہ کیا جائے۔

ات يقاسم : باب مفاعلت سے ہے آپس میں تقسیم کرنا۔
The grandfather has more right to inheritance than the brothers according to Abu Hanifa. It is difficult to take back wealth from heirs twice. Therefore, to be cautious, the son's share is kept aside so that wealth does not have to be taken back from heirs. And if a daughter is born, after giving her share, whatever remains will be given later to the remaining heirs. The fetus becomes an heir; its proof is this hadith that has passed: From Abu Huraira from the Prophet (s) said: 'When the newborn cries out, he inherits' (Abu Dawood, chapter on the newborn crying then dying, p. 49, no. 2920; Ibn Majah, chapter on prayer for the abortus, vol. 2, p. 215, no. 1508). From this hadith it is known that if the child is born alive, he will be an heir. From this it is also established that if someone dies after the pregnancy is confirmed, his inheritance will go to the fetus and wealth will be set aside for it. [13239 (11)] The grandfather has more right to inheritance than the brothers according to Abu Hanifa. And the two Sahibayn say that he will share equally with the brothers, unless he gets less than one-third in the division. According to Abu Hanifa, the grandfather is given precedence over the brothers, so he will be given first; whatever remains will then be given to the brothers. The two Sahibayn say: after the entitled shares, whatever wealth remains, both will share half and half. However, if in dividing half and half, the grandfather gets less than one-third, then the grandfather will first be given one-third, then whatever remains will be given to the brother. Abu Hanifa's proof is this athar: Abu Bakr, Ibn Abbas, and Ibn al-Zubayr said, 'The grandfather is a father,' and Ibn Abbas recited 'O children of Adam' (Surah al-A'raf, 7:26) and 'I followed the religion of my fathers Ibrahim, Ishaq, and Ya'qub' (Surah Yusuf, 12:38) (Bukhari, chapter on inheritance of grandfather with father and brothers, p. 997, no. 6737). In this athar, the grandfather is placed in the position of a father because in the verse, Prophet Ya'qub is called a father even though he was a grandfather. This shows that the grandfather is in the rank of a father. And the brother is excluded by the father, so the brother is also excluded by the grandfather. (2) The hadith shows the importance of the grandfather: from Imran ibn Husayn that a man came to the Prophet (s) and said: 'My grandson died; what do I get from his inheritance?' He said: 'You get one-sixth.' When he turned away, he called him and said: 'You get another sixth.' When he turned away, he called him and said: 'The other sixth is an extra share (tu'ma).' (Abu Dawood, chapter on inheritance of grandfather, p. 45, no. 2896). In this hadith, the grandfather was given a share and also an additional sixth as an asabah, so the grandfather has importance over the brother. The proof of the Sahibayn is this athar: from al-Zuhri: Umar ibn al-Khattab used to share between the grandfather and the brother when there were no others, and he gave the grandfather one-third with two brothers, and whenever dividing was better, he would divide and not let the grandfather get less than one-sixth from all the wealth. (Another narration: the two caliphs before you, meaning Umar and Uthman, used to decree for the grandfather with one brother half, with two brothers one-third, and if there were more, they would not reduce from one-third.) (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, chapter on the prescribed share of the grandfather, vol. 10, p. 266, nos. 1906, 19062; Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, vol. 6, p. 262, no. 31215). From this athar, it is understood that the grandfather will be given half of the brother's share after division. However, if in the division he gets less than one-third, then the grandfather will be given one-third, because in the other athar it says: 'He did not reduce from one-third.' 'Iqasama' is from the pattern of mufa'alah, meaning mutual division.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage from 'Bāb al-Radd' (Chapter of Return) presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the inheritance of the grandfather (jadd) when siblings (ikhwa) are present. The author begins by noting that it is difficult to recover wealth from heirs after distribution, so precaution is taken by reserving the share of an unborn child (fetus) if one exists, based on a hadith that a live-born child is an heir. Then the main issue is introduced: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the grandfather is more entitled to inheritance than the siblings, meaning he takes the entire estate (after fixed Quranic shares) and excludes siblings. The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the grandfather shares equally with siblings, except that his share should not fall below one-third of the estate. Author then explains the positions in detail: Abu Hanifa says grandfather takes precedence; siblings get only what remains. Sahibayn say after fixed shares, grandfather and siblings split the residue equally (half each), but if that gives grandfather less than one-third, he gets one-third first. Abu Hanifa's proof consists of two athar: (1) Companions (Abu Bakr, Ibn Abbas, Ibn al-Zubayr) called the grandfather a 'father' (ab), using Quranic evidence where Prophet Ya'qub is called father even though he was a grandfather, so the grandfather is in the position of a father, and just as brothers are excluded by father, they are excluded by grandfather. (2) A hadith where the Prophet gave the grandfather two sixths: one as a prescribed share and one as an asabah, indicating the grandfather's precedence over siblings. Sahibayn's proof is athar from Umar and Uthman that they used to make the grandfather share with siblings, giving him half with one brother, one-third with two, and never less than one-third regardless of number of siblings. The author concludes that the effect of Sahibayn's evidence is that grandfather gets half of what the brother gets after division, but if that share is less than one-third, he gets one-third. The language note clarifies that 'yaqāsimu' means mutual division. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa, as he is the Imam and his proofs are considered stronger.

Key Terms
جد (jadd): The paternal grandfather of the deceased (father's father).
صاحبین (Sahibayn): The two main disciples of Abu Hanifa: Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
اثر (athar): A narration from a Companion (Sahabi) of the Prophet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the position of the grandfather (jadd) when siblings are also heirs?
A) He shares equally with siblings.
B) He is excluded by siblings.
C) He takes precedence over siblings and excludes them.
D) He inherits one-sixth and the rest goes to siblings.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that the paternal grandfather excludes siblings from inheritance, based on his analogy to a father; the Sahibayn's contrary view is acknowledged but not followed for fatwa.

Inheritance of Multiple Grandmothers
Ruling on the sixth share for the nearest grandmother(s)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for distributing the sixth share among multiple grandmothers, identifying the closest heir(s) and calculating their share.

Core Idea
Only the nearest grandmother(s) inherit the sixth; distant ones are excluded.

In Hanafi faraid, when multiple grandmothers survive the deceased, the fixed sixth share does not belong to all of them equally. Instead, the school applies a rule of proximity: only the grandmother(s) closest in lineage to the deceased receive the share. This lesson unpacks the rule, the proofs from the early community, and how to distribute the sixth when grandmothers of equal degree claim it.

Guided Path
1
List all surviving grandmothers and their degrees
Identify each grandmother by lineage type (paternal or maternal) and generational distance from the deceased. For example, a paternal grandmother (father's mother) is one degree away; a great maternal grandmother (mother's mother's mother) is three degrees away.
Hanafi law recognizes up to three grandmothers.
2
Determine the nearest degree(s)
Compare the degrees of all grandmothers. The closest degree (smallest number of generations) is the nearest. If there are multiple grandmothers at the same nearest degree, they are all equally near.
For example, a paternal grandmother and a maternal grandmother are both one degree away.
3
Apply hajb hirman (total exclusion)
Any grandmother whose degree is further than the nearest degree is completely excluded from inheritance. They receive nothing.
The nearest degree blocks all more distant ones.
4
Distribute the sixth among the nearest grandmothers
If there is only one grandmother at the nearest degree, she receives the full sixth. If there are multiple at that degree, they share the sixth equally.
The total share allocated to grandmothers is always one-sixth of the estate.
5
Calculate the final shares
Divide the sixth by the number of nearest grandmothers. For example, if two equally near grandmothers, each gets 1/12 of the estate.
No further adjustments (awl or radd) are needed in this distribution.

Source Text

واذا اجتمع الجدات فالسدس لاقربهن. مثلا دادی بھی ہے اور پر دادی بھی ہے نانی بھی ہے اور پر نانی بھی ہے ایسی صورت میں جو دادی یا نانی قریبی ہے اس کو چھٹا حصہ ملے گا اور جو دور کی ہے اس کو نہیں ملے گا۔ مثلا دادی ہے اور پر نانی ہے تو دادی کو چھٹا حصہ ملے گا اور پر نانی کو کچھ نہیں ملے گا۔ کیونکہ وہ دور کی نانی ہے۔

اثر میں ہے۔ عن الحسن انه كان يورث ثلاث جدات ويقول ايتهن كانت اقرب فهو لها دون الأخرى فاذا استوتا فهو بينهما (ج) (مصنف ابن ابی شیبہ ۲۲ فی الحدات لم ترث منھن ، ج سادس، ص ۲۷۲، نمبر ۳۱۲۷۵ / مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب فرض الجدات ، ج عاشر ص ۲۷۶ نمبر ۱۹۰۸۹) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ جو دادی یا نانی قریب ہو اس کو ملے گا ۔ اور اگر دونوں برابر درجے کے ہوں تو چھٹا حصہ دونوں میں تقسیم ہو گا۔

اثر میں ہے۔ ثم جاءت الجدة الاخرى الى عمر بن الخطاب تسأله ميراثها ... وما انا بزائد في الفرائض ولكن هو ذلك السدس فان اجتمعتما فيه فهو بينكما وايتكما مخلت به فهو لها (و) (ابوداؤد شریف ، باب فی الجدة ، ص ۴۵
And when grandmothers gather, the sixth is for the closest among them. For example, there is a paternal grandmother (dadi) and a great paternal grandmother (par dadi), a maternal grandmother (nani) and a great maternal grandmother (par nani). In such a case, whichever grandmother or maternal grandmother is closer will get the sixth share, and the distant one will not get anything. For example, there is a paternal grandmother and a great maternal grandmother: the paternal grandmother will get the sixth, and the great maternal grandmother will get nothing because she is distant. In an athar: from al-Hasan (al-Basri) that he used to make three grandmothers inherit and said: whichever of them is closer, it (the sixth) is for her, not the other; if they are equal, then it is between them. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, vol. 6, p. 272, no. 31275; Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, vol. 10, p. 276, no. 19089). From this athar, it is understood that whichever grandmother or maternal grandmother is closer will get it, and if both are of equal degree, the sixth share will be divided between them. And in an athar: 'Then the other grandmother came to Umar ibn al-Khattab asking for her inheritance... and (Umar said): I am not one to add to the prescribed shares, but that is the sixth; if you both gather (as the only grandmothers), it is between you, and whichever of you is alone with it, it is for her.' (Abu Dawood, chapter on the grandmother, p. 45)
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage from the sharh states the ruling on the inheritance of grandmothers (jaddāt) when multiple are present. The rule is: the sixth share (one-sixth of the estate) belongs to the nearest grandmother (in terms of lineage distance from the deceased). The author gives examples: if a paternal grandmother (dadi) and a great maternal grandmother (par nani) both survive, the paternal grandmother takes the whole sixth because she is closer; the great maternal grandmother is excluded because she is further away. The same applies if the closeness is equal, e.g., two grandmothers of the same degree (both maternal or both paternal of the same level), they share the sixth equally. Two proofs (athar) are cited: first, a narration from Hasan al-Basri that he used to assign inheritance to three grandmothers (though only up to three can inherit) and gave the sixth to the closest, or if equal, share it. Second, an incident with Umar ibn al-Khattab where he stated he would not change the fixed shares (Quranic sixths) but if two grandmothers of equal degree are present, they share the sixth; if only one, she gets it all. This establishes that the fixed sixth for grandmothers does not increase; it is distributed among those of equal nearness.

Key Terms
جدات (jaddāt): Grandmothers, including both paternal and maternal grandmothers and great-grandmothers.
سدس (suds): The fixed share of one-sixth prescribed for grandmothers and other heirs in Qur'an.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a paternal grandmother (dadi) and a great maternal grandmother (par nani) survive the deceased, who inherits the sixth share?
A) The paternal grandmother gets the full sixth.
B) The great maternal grandmother gets the full sixth.
C) They share the sixth equally.
D) Both are excluded because the sixth goes to the grandfather.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi faraid, multiple grandmothers do not share the sixth equally; only the nearest degree inherits, and if equally near, they divide the sixth equally.

Inheritance of Multiple Grandmothers: The Rule of the Nearest
Ruling on distribution of sixth among multiple grandmothers based on proximity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for distributing the sixth among multiple grandmothers and apply it to determine which grandmother inherits.

Core Idea
Only the nearest grandmother inherits the sixth; distant ones are excluded unless equally near.

This passage from the chapter of Radd addresses a common inheritance scenario: when multiple grandmothers survive the deceased. The Hanafi school uses proximity to the deceased as the key criterion, supported by traditions from the Companions. You will learn the rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grandmothers
List all female ancestors: paternal and maternal, distinguishing between true (haqiqiyya) and false (hukmiyya).
True grandmothers are those without a male/female intervening ancestor; others are distant.
2
Apply the proximity rule
The nearest grandmother in lineage excludes all more distant ones. If two are equally near, they share the sixth equally.
Proximity is measured by the number of generational steps to the deceased.
3
Consider the evidence
The rule is based on athar (companion practice) from Hasan al-Basri and Umar ibn al-Khattab, not a direct Quranic verse.
These athar explicitly state the nearness condition.
4
Compare with Shafi'i school
Shafi'i gives the sixth to any grandmother without the nearness condition; Hanafi differs based on transmitted practice.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Chapter of Return. (12) And when grandmothers gather, the sixth is for the nearest among them. For example, there is a paternal grandmother and a great-paternal grandmother, and a maternal grandmother and a great-maternal grandmother. In such a case, whichever grandmother is nearer gets the sixth share, and the distant one does not get anything. For example, there is a paternal grandmother and a great-maternal grandmother: the paternal grandmother gets the sixth, and the great-maternal grandmother gets nothing because she is distant. There is an athar from al-Hasan that he used to give inheritance to three grandmothers and said: whichever of them is nearer, it is for her to the exclusion of the other; if they are equal, it is between them. This athar shows that the nearer grandmother gets it, and if both are of equal degree, the sixth is divided between them. Another athar: Then another grandmother came to 'Umar ibn al-Khattab asking for her inheritance... and he said: I am not one to increase the shares, but it is that sixth; if you both gather in it, it is between you; whichever of you is alone with it, it is for her. This athar shows that if several grandmothers gather, the sixth is divided among them all.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the inheritance of grandmothers. The ruling states that when multiple grandmothers are present, only the nearest grandmother in lineage inherits the sixth share. If two are equally near, they divide the sixth equally. This is supported by two athar (traditions from the Companions). The first from Hasan al-Basri indicates that the nearer grandmother excludes the more distant one, but if they are equal, they share. The second from 'Umar ibn al-Khattab shows that when multiple grandmothers come together, the sixth is distributed among them. This is a standard Hanafi ruling: grandmothers of equal degree share the sixth proportionally.

Key Terms
جدة (jaddah): Grandmother; a female ancestor in the paternal or maternal line.
سدس (suds): One-sixth; the fixed Quranic share for grandmothers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a deceased leaves two grandmothers of equal proximity (e.g., paternal and maternal grandmothers), how is the sixth divided?
A) The paternal grandmother gets the entire sixth.
B) The maternal grandmother gets the entire sixth.
C) They share the sixth equally.
D) The sixth is divided proportionally based on lineage.

Key Takeaway
The sixth for grandmothers goes to the nearest; if equally near, they share.

The Proof for Grandmothers' Inheritance: The Athar from Hasan and Umar
Ruling on distribution of sixth among multiple grandmothers based on proximity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the athar used as proof for the Hanafi grandmother inheritance rule and explain their epistemological weight.

Core Idea
The Hanafi rule for grandmothers rests on the practice of the Companions (athar), not direct Quranic text.

While the Quran specifies shares for daughters, mothers, etc., the grandmother's share is derived from the Sunnah and the practice of the Companions. This lesson examines two key athar transmitted by Hasan al-Basri and 'Umar ibn al-Khattab that establish the proximity condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source
Recognize that the ruling is based on athar (narrations from Companions), not a Quranic verse or Prophetic hadith.
The first athar is from Hasan al-Basri, the second from 'Umar.
2
Understand the content
Both athar explicitly state the nearness condition: the nearest grandmother gets the sixth; if equal, they share.
The wording 'ايتهن كانت اقرب فهو لها' indicates exclusivity.
3
Assess epistemological weight
These are narrations with a chain (isnad); they are considered zanni al-thubut but were widely practiced.
Hanafi school accepts mursal narrations from reliable Tabi'un when corroborated by companion practice.
4
Compare with Shafi'i
Shafi'i does not restrict to the nearest, relying on analogy rather than these specific athar.
This shows the impact of evidence preference on rulings.

Hajb of Grandmothers: Three Exclusion Rules in Hanafi Faraid
Rulings on exclusion (hajb) of grandmothers by closer relatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three rules of exclusion regarding grandmothers in Hanafi inheritance law and apply them to determine which grandmother is excluded in a given heir set.

Core Idea
Every grandmother can be excluded by a closer relative; the grandfather excludes his mother, and a grandmother excludes her own mother.

This lesson examines three specific rulings from Al-Sharh al-Thamiri on the exclusion of grandmothers in inheritance. These rules illustrate the Hanafi principle that inheritance shares are determined by closeness of relation, and a nearer relative blocks a more distant one from receiving any portion. Understanding these exclusions is essential for correctly calculating shares when multiple generations of female ancestors are present.

Guided Path
1
Identify the excluded and excluding heir in each rule
For ruling (13): the grandfather excludes his mother (great-grandmother). For (14): the mother of the father's mother (maternal great-grandmother) is excluded because her son is a dhu al-rahim. For (15): every grandmother excludes her own mother (great-grandmother from that side).
Focus on the relationship: the excluding heir is always closer to the deceased.
2
Determine the effective cause (illa) for exclusion
The illa is closeness of kinship. The grandfather, as an asabah, blocks his mother. The maternal grandfather’s mother is excluded because he is not an asabah. The grandmother excludes her mother due to being one degree closer.
The illa is consistent: a nearer relative prevents a more distant one from inheriting.
3
Apply the rules to a family tree scenario
Consider a deceased with a mother, paternal grandmother, and maternal grandmother. The mother excludes both grandmothers (per the hadith). This scenario tests your ability to identify the excluding heir.
Check if the excluding heir is present before applying the rule.
4
Examine the hadith proof for the principle
The hadith from Abu Dawud states the grandmother receives the sixth only when there is no mother below her. This directly establishes the mother's exclusion of grandmothers.
The hadith is a key proof for the hierarchical exclusion among female ancestors.
5
Summarize the hierarchical principle
In Hanafi faraid, the closer relative in the female line (mother, grandmother, etc.) excludes the more distant one. The grandfather also excludes his mother. The share of a grandmother is only available when no closer ancestor (mother or paternal grandfather) exists.
This principle is fundamental for solving inheritance problems involving grandmothers.

Al-Sharh al-Thamiri, Part 4, page 439. Chapter of Return. [3241] (13) And the grandfather excludes his mother. [3242] (14) And the mother of the father's mother does not inherit anything. [3243] (15) And every grandmother excludes her mother. Number 2894 / Tirmidhi Sharif, chapter on inheritance of grandmother, page 30, number 2100: In this report, when several paternal grandmothers or several maternal grandmothers gather, the sixth part is distributed among them all. Principle: For all paternal and maternal grandmothers, only the sixth part applies. [3241] (13) The grandfather will exclude his mother. When the grandfather is present, his mother gets nothing. The grandfather himself is an asabah, so he takes all the property. Therefore, what will the one after him get? (3242] (14) The mother of the mother's father will not inherit anything. That is, the mother of the mother's father is the mother's paternal grandmother and the deceased's great-grandmother. In this, the maternal grandfather is a dhu al-rahim, and she is the mother of the maternal grandfather. Since a dhu al-rahim is not an asabah, how can his mother become an asabah? And she will not get any share. (3243) (15) Every grandmother excludes her mother. The grandmother is in the position of the mother. And when the mother is present, the paternal or maternal grandmother gets nothing; she excludes them. Similarly, the grandmother excludes her own mother. There is a hadith: From Ibn Burayda from his father that the Prophet, peace be upon him, assigned the sixth to the grandmother when there is no mother below her. (Abu Dawud Sharif, chapter on the grandmother, page 45, number 2895) This hadith shows that when a mother is present, the grandmother gets nothing, because the mother excludes the grandmother. Similarly, when the grandmother is present, she excludes her own mother. Footnote: (A) He assigned the sixth to the grandmother if there is no mother.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh al-Thamiri expands on three rulings regarding the exclusion (hajb) of grandmothers in inheritance. Ruling (13): The grandfather (paternal grandfather) excludes his own mother (the great-grandmother of the deceased) because the grandfather is an asabah and takes the entire estate, leaving nothing for his mother. Ruling (14): The mother of the father's mother (i.e., the maternal grandmother of the deceased's mother) does not inherit at all. This is because her son (the maternal grandfather of the deceased) is from the category of dhawi al-arham (uterine relatives), who do not inherit when there is an asabah. Since the maternal grandfather is not an asabah, his mother cannot be an heir either. Ruling (15): Every grandmother excludes her own mother. This is analogous to the rule that a mother excludes her own mother (the maternal grandmother) when she is present; similarly, a grandmother excludes her mother (her own mother) from inheritance. The hadith cited from Abu Dawood confirms that a grandmother receives the sixth only if there is no mother below her, establishing the principle that a closer mother excludes a more distant grandmother. These rulings are part of the standard Hanafi faraid and are found in the zahir al-riwayah works. They reflect the hierarchical order of exclusion based on closeness of relationship.

Key Terms
حجب (hajb): The legal exclusion of an heir from inheritance due to the presence of a closer relative.
جد (jadd): The paternal grandfather of the deceased.
جدة (jaddah): A grandmother, either paternal (dadi) or maternal (nani).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi inheritance rulings, whom does the grandfather (jadd) exclude?
A) His own mother
B) His paternal grandmother
C) His daughter
D) His wife

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi inheritance, grandmothers are excluded by closer relatives: the mother excludes all grandmothers, the grandfather excludes his mother, and a grandmother excludes her own mother.

The Proof for Inheritance of Dhawi al-Arham
Establishes the basis for dhawi al-arham inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi proofs (Quran and hadith) for dhawi al-arham inheritance and explain why the Hanafi position overrides the alternative view of placing the estate in the public treasury.

Core Idea
Dhawi al-arham inherit when no closer heirs exist — Quran and hadith establish their right.

In the Hanafi school, distant kindred (dhawi al-arham) inherit after the primary sharers and residuaries are exhausted. This lesson examines the textual evidence that supports their entitlement: a Quranic verse and a prophetic hadith. We also consider a dissenting opinion from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, which the Hanafi school set aside in favor of the stronger proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage establishes that when there are no ashab al-furud or asabah, the estate goes to dhawi al-arham, not to the public treasury.
This is the core Hanafi position.
2
Examine the primary dalil: Quran 8:75
The verse 'And those of kinship are more worthy of each other' is cited. The word 'أولو الأرحام' includes all blood relatives, and 'أولى' indicates entitlement to inheritance.
Hanafi usul: the general import (umum) of the verse covers dhawi al-arham.
3
Examine the secondary dalil: the hadith of al-Miqdam
The hadith explicitly makes the maternal uncle an heir when there is no other heir. Since the maternal uncle is a dhawi al-rahm, the ruling extends by analogy to all dhawi al-arham.
This hadith reinforces the Quranic evidence.
4
Consider the dissenting view of Ibn Mas'ud
Ibn Mas'ud reported a case where he placed the estate in the public treasury when no close heirs existed. His view would deny dhawi al-arham inheritance.
This is an early difference of opinion.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preference)
The Hanafi school prefers the Quran and hadith over Ibn Mas'ud's athar, because the revealed texts are stronger evidence. The athar is interpreted as cautionary or specific to that context.
The mufta bihi position is that dhawi al-arham inherit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ذوی الارحام کے ضروری نوٹ وہ قریبی رشتہ دار جو نہ حصے والے ہوں اور نہ عصبہ ہو کہ حصہ لینے کے بعد یہ تمام مال جمع کر لے ان کو ذوی الارحام کہتے ہیں۔ اگر سہام والے بھی ہوں اور عصبہ بھی نہ ہوں تو مال ذوی الارحام کو دیا جائے گا۔ اسکا ثبوت اس آیت میں ہے۔ واولوا الا رخام بعضهم أولى ببعض فى كتاب الله ان الله بكل شيء عليم (الف ) ( آیت ۷۵، سورۃ الانفال ۸ ) اس آیت میں ہے کہ ذوی الارحام وراثت کے لئے زیادہ بہتر ہے۔ اس لئے عصبہ نہ ہو تو بیت المال میں داخل کرنے کے بجائے ذوی الارحام کو دیا جائے گا (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن المقدام قال قال رسول الله الا الله ... والخال وارث من لا وارث له يعقل عنه ويرثه (ب) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب ماجاء فی میراث ذوی الارحام، ص ۳۰، نمبر ۲۸۹۹ / ترندی شریف، باب ما جاء فی میراث الخال ،ص۳۰، نمبر ۲۱۰۳) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ کوئی وارث نہ ہو تو ماموں وارث ہے۔ اور ماموں ذوی الارحام میں سے ہیں۔ جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ ذوی الارحام وارث ہوں گے۔ عبد اللہ بن مسعود کی رائے ہے کہ عصبہ نہ ہو تو ذوی الارحام کو نہ دیا جائے بلکہ بیت المال میں داخل کر دیا جائے۔ ان کا اثر یہ ہے۔ عن مسروق قال اتيت عبد الله یعنی ابن مسعود فقلت ان رجلا كان فينا نازلا فخرج الى الجبل فمات وترث ثلث مائة درهم فقال عبد الله هل ترك وارثا او لاحد منكم عليه عقد ولاء ؟ قلت لا قال له ههنا ورثة كثير فجعل ماله في بیت المال (ج) (سن للمبتی ، باب من جعل میراث من لم يدع وار ناولا مولی فی بیت المال، ج سادس ،ص ۳۹۹ نمبر ۱۲۴۰۴ مصنف ابن ابي شيبة ، ۶۸ من قال للملاعیہ المثلث و مابقی فی بیت المال، ج سادس ، ص ۲۷۶، نمبر ۳۱۳۱۴) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ عصبہ نہ ہو تو مال بیت المال میں داخل کر دیا جائے تا کہ عام مسلمانوں کومل جائے۔
Chapter of Dhawi al-Arham. Essential Note: Those close relatives who are neither share-holders nor 'asaba, such that after receiving shares, they take the entire property; they are called dhawi al-arham. If there are share-holders but no 'asaba, then the property will be given to dhawi al-arham. Its proof is in this verse: 'And those of kinship are more worthy of each other in the Book of Allah; indeed Allah is Knowing of all things.' (Quran 8:75). In this verse, it states that dhawi al-arham are better for inheritance. Therefore, if there is no 'asaba, it will be given to dhawi al-arham instead of putting it in the public treasury. (2) There is a hadith: From Miqdam who said: The Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'Indeed, the maternal uncle inherits from one who has no heir; he pays his blood money and inherits from him.' (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi) This hadith states that if there is no heir, the maternal uncle is an heir. And the maternal uncle is from among dhawi al-arham. From this it is understood that dhawi al-arham will inherit. Abdullah ibn Mas'ud held the opinion that if there is no 'asaba, it should not be given to dhawi al-arham but should be put in the public treasury. His athar is: From Masruq: I came to Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and said: A man was staying with us as a guest; he went to the mountain and died, leaving three hundred dirhams. Abdullah said: Did he leave an heir or do any of you have a contract of wala? I said: No. He said: There are many heirs here (i.e., the public treasury). So he put his wealth in the public treasury. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba) From this athar, it is understood that if there is no 'asaba, the wealth should be placed in the public treasury so that it reaches all Muslims.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Dhawi al-Arham, the distant kindred who inherit in the absence of primary heirs (ashab al-furud) and residuaries (asabat). The author first defines the term and then provides textual proofs. The primary evidence from the Quran is verse 8:75, which declares that blood relatives are more entitled to inheritance than others. This verse is interpreted in the Hanafi school to include all relatives, not just those with prescribed shares or residuary rights. The hadith of al-Miqdam explicitly makes the maternal uncle an heir when there is no other, and since the maternal uncle is a dhawi al-rahm, the ruling extends by analogy to all such relatives. The author also cites the view of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud that the estate should go to the public treasury, which represents an early difference of opinion. However, the Hanafi school follows the majority view (based on the Quran and hadith) that dhawi al-arham inherit. The commentary on the margin (حاشية) provides source references for clarity.

Key Terms
ذوی الارحام (dhawi al-arham): Relatives who are neither ashab al-furud nor asabah, but inherit in their absence.
عصبہ (asabah): Agnatic male relatives who inherit as residuaries.
بیت المال (bayt al-mal): Public treasury of the Muslim state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary proof for dhawi al-arham inheritance?
A) The practice of Abu Bakr
B) Quran 8:75 and the hadith of al-Miqdam
C) The consensus of the Companions
D) Qiyas on the inheritance of the wife

Key Takeaway
Dhawi al-arham inherit in the Hanafi school when no ashab al-furud or asabah exist, based on Quran 8:75 and the hadith of al-Miqdam, and their right takes precedence over the public treasury.

The Ten Categories of Dhawi al-Arham
Lists the ten groups of distant kindred who inherit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten categories of dhawi al-arham and identify which relatives fall into each category.

Core Idea
Dhawi al-arham are ten categories of distant relatives who inherit only when no 'asaba or dhawi al-furud exist.

This passage from Al-Sharh al-Sameri enumerates the ten groups of relatives known as dhawi al-arham. In Hanafi faraid, when the deceased has no agnatic heirs ('asaba) and no fixed-share heirs (dhawi al-furud), inheritance passes to these distant kindred. Understanding this list is essential for correct distribution in residual cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for dhawi al-arham inheritance
The passage states that dhawi al-arham inherit only when there is no 'asaba and no dhawi al-furud. Understand that these are residual heirs.
Primary heirs always take priority.
2
List the ten categories
Memorize the ten categories: child of daughter, child of sister, daughter of brother, daughter of paternal uncle, maternal uncle, maternal aunt, maternal grandfather, paternal uncle through mother, paternal aunt, child of brother from mother.
Group them by relation type for easier recall.
3
Distinguish each category from primary heirs
Note that these are distant relatives: e.g., child of daughter is a grandchild through a female line, unlike a son who is an 'asaba.
Primary heirs include children, parents, siblings, etc.
4
Apply the list in a hypothetical scenario
Given a deceased with no asaba or dhawi al-furud, identify which of these categories are present and thus inherit.
Remember that if any primary heir exists, dhawi al-arham are excluded.
5
Check for common errors
Avoid confusion: the paternal uncle (full) is an 'asaba, not a dhawi al-rahm. The list includes only specific relatives.
Refer to the list carefully.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا لم يكن للميت عصبة ولا دوسهم ورثه ذوو الارحام وهم عشرة ولد البنت وولد الاخت وبنت الاخ وبنت العم والخال والخالة وابو الام والعم لام والعمة وولد الاخ من الام
And if the deceased has no 'asaba and no dhawi al-furud, then his heirs are dhawi al-arham, and they are ten: the child of the daughter, the child of the sister, the daughter of the brother, the daughter of the paternal uncle, the maternal uncle, the maternal aunt, the maternal grandfather, the paternal uncle through the mother, the paternal aunt, and the child of the brother from the mother.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage provides a concise list of the ten categories of dhawi al-arham who inherit in the absence of primary heirs. These relatives are connected through female links or through non-agnatic channels. The list includes: (1) grandchildren through a daughter (son's daughter or daughter's daughter), (2) children of a sister (nephew/niece), (3) daughter of a brother, (4) daughter of a paternal uncle (cousin), (5) maternal uncle, (6) maternal aunt, (7) maternal grandfather, (8) paternal uncle through the mother (half-paternal uncle by mother), (9) paternal aunt, and (10) children of a half-brother from the mother. These are all considered dhawi al-arham because they do not inherit as ashab al-furud or asabat in the standard scheme.

Key Terms
ذوی الارحام (dhawi al-arham): The ten categories of distant kindred listed here who inherit in the absence of primary heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Maternal uncle
D) Paternal aunt

Key Takeaway
Memorize the ten categories of dhawi al-arham to correctly identify heirs when primary heirs are absent.

Priority Among Dhawi al-Arham by Lineage
Ruling statement on priority order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three-tier priority order among dhawi al-arham and identify the types of relatives in each tier.

Core Idea
Inheritance among distant relatives follows the chain of lineage: first grandchildren, then nieces/nephews, then aunts/uncles.

This passage lays down the Hanafi rule for prioritizing dhawi al-arham when no primary heirs exist. The principle is straightforward: the closer the genealogical link to the deceased, the higher the priority. We will break down the three categories and understand the specific relatives included in each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three tiers
Listen to the order: first, those from the deceased's children; second, those from the deceased's parents; third, those from the deceased's grandparents.
The order is always based on proximity to the deceased in the lineage.
2
Understand 'min walad al-mayyit'
This refers to the children of the deceased themselves, but in the context of dhawi al-arham, it means grandchildren through daughters, since sons' children are asabah, not dhawi al-arham.
Grandchildren from a son are asabah and inherit as such; only grandchildren through the daughter are dhawi al-arham.
3
Understand 'walad al-abawayn'
These are the children of the deceased's parents, i.e., the deceased's siblings' children. The text specifies 'daughters of brothers and children of sisters'.
Sons of brothers are asabah and not dhawi al-arham; daughters of brothers and all children of sisters are dhawi al-arham.
4
Understand 'walad abaway abayhi'
These are the children of the deceased's grandparents, i.e., the deceased's uncles and aunts. The text mentions maternal uncles, maternal aunts, and paternal aunts.
Paternal uncles are asabah, so they are not included here; only the uncles and aunts from the mother's side and the paternal aunt are dhawi al-arham.
5
Memorize the examples
For the second tier: 'banat al-ikhwan' (brothers' daughters) and 'awlad al-akhawat' (sisters' children). For the third: 'al-akhwal' (maternal uncles), 'al-khalat' (maternal aunts), 'al-ammat' (paternal aunts).
These examples help in identifying which relatives fall into each tier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من ادلى بهم فاولهم من كان من ولد الميت ثم ولد الابوين او احدهما وهم بنات الاخوة واولاد الاخوات ثم ولد ابوى ابويه او احدهما وهم الاخوال والخالات والعمات
And whoever is related through them, the foremost of them is whoever is from the children of the deceased, then the children of both parents or one of them, and they are the daughters of brothers and the children of sisters, then the children of both parents of his parents or one of them, and they are the maternal uncles, maternal aunts, and paternal aunts.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage establishes the order of inheritance among dhawi al-arham based on their genealogical proximity to the deceased. The principle is that those who are closer in lineage take precedence. The three tiers are: (1) descendants of the deceased himself, meaning grandchildren through daughters (e.g., son's daughter or daughter's daughter). (2) descendants of the deceased's parents, meaning children of the deceased's siblings (i.e., nieces and nephews through sisters, and daughters of brothers). (3) descendants of the deceased's grandparents, meaning the deceased's uncles and aunts on both sides (maternal uncles, maternal aunts, paternal aunts). The paternal uncle's daughter was listed separately in the previous lesson but she falls under this third tier.

Key Terms
ولد الميت (walad al-mayyit): Children of the deceased, here specifically grandchildren through the daughter.
ولد الابوين (walad al-abawayn): Children of the deceased's parents, i.e., the deceased's siblings' children.
ولد ابوى ابويه (walad abaway abayhi): Children of the deceased's grandparents, i.e., the deceased's uncles and aunts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who inherits first among dhawi al-arham?
A) Children of the deceased's siblings
B) Children of the deceased's grandparents
C) Children of the deceased himself (grandchildren)
D) The public treasury

Key Takeaway
The priority among dhawi al-arham is based on the closeness of their lineage to the deceased: first grandchildren, then nieces/nephews, then aunts/uncles.

Three Tiers of Dhawi al-Arham Priority
States the general order of inheritance among dhawi al-arham

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three-tier priority order among dhawi al-arham and identify which relatives belong to each tier.

Core Idea
Dhawi al-arham inherit in three tiers: first the deceased's descendants, then siblings' descendants, then grandparents' descendants.

In the Hanafi school, when there are no fixed-share heirs (ashab al-fara'id) or residuary takers (asabat), the estate passes to dhawi al-arham—blood relatives who are not included in those categories. To determine who inherits, the Hanafi jurists established a clear priority order based on closeness to the deceased. This lesson explains that order and the Qur'anic proof behind it.

Guided Path
1
Define dhawi al-arham and understand their role
Learn the definition of dhawi al-arham (uterine relatives) and when they inherit: only after ashab al-fara'id and asabat are exhausted. This sets the context for the priority order.
Dhawi al-arham include grandchildren, nieces, nephews, maternal uncles, etc.
2
Identify the three tiers in order
Memorize the three tiers: (1) the deceased's own descendants (e.g., grandchildren), (2) descendants of the deceased's parents (e.g., nephews/nieces), (3) descendants of the deceased's grandparents (e.g., maternal uncles, paternal aunts). Within each tier, proximity in degree matters.
The text gives examples: first tier = children's children; second = siblings' children; third = grandparents' children.
3
Apply the Qur'anic proof
Analyze the verse 'وَأُولُو الْأَرْحَامِ بَعْضُهُمْ أَوْلَىٰ بِبَعْضٍ' (Al-Anfal 8:75) to see how 'أولىٰ' indicates priority, establishing the basis for this hierarchy.
The verse is general, but the Hanafi school interprets it to mean that closer relatives are preferred over distant ones.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Understand that the Shafi'i school does not recognize inheritance of dhawi al-arham; instead, the estate goes to bayt al-mal (public treasury). This highlights a major ikhtilaf.
The Hanafi position is based on a broader interpretation of the verse and Hadith.

Chapter of Dhawi al-Arham: The daughter, sister's child, brother's daughter, paternal uncle's daughter, maternal uncle, maternal aunt, maternal grandfather, paternal uncle through mother, paternal aunt, and brother through mother. And whoever is connected through them: the first of them is whoever is from the deceased's child, then the child of both parents or one of them, and they are the daughters of brothers and children of sisters, then the child of the grandparents or one of them, and they are maternal uncles, maternal aunts, and paternal aunts. And if the children of a father are equal in degree, the first of them is... [Urdu explanation of the ten degrees and supporting athar and Quran verse 8:75]
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage states the general order of inheritance among dhawi al-arham (uterine relatives). The ruling is that they are prioritized in three tiers: first, the deceased's own descendants (e.g., grandchildren); second, the descendants of the deceased's parents (e.g., nephews and nieces); third, the descendants of the deceased's grandparents (e.g., maternal uncles, paternal aunts). Within each tier, those closer in degree inherit before those farther. The proof is the Qur'anic verse: 'But those of [blood] relationship are more entitled [to inheritance] in the decree of Allah' (al-Anfal 8:75). The athar of Ali and Zayd regarding grandmothers reinforces the principle that the closer relative takes precedence.

Key Terms
ذوي الارحام (dhawi al-arham): Relatives who are neither fixed-share heirs (ashab al-fara'id) nor residuary takers (asabat), such as grandchildren, nieces, and cousins.
ولد الابوين (walad al-abawayn): Children of both parents, i.e., full siblings or their descendants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi priority among dhawi al-arham follows three tiers: first, the deceased's descendants; second, descendants of parents; third, descendants of grandparents.

Priority Among Equal-Degree Dhawi al-Arham
States a priority rule for dhawi al-arham

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of priority among equal-degree dhawi al-arham by identifying which relative is connected through an inheritor.

Core Idea
Among dhawi al-arham of equal degree, the one linked through a legal inheritor inherits first.

This passage from the Hanafi faraid text establishes a crucial qualifying rule for dhawi al-arham inheritance. When two distant relatives are equally close by degree, the one whose connecting parent or grandparent is an actual inheritor (asabah or fixed-share heir) takes precedence. The examples and the athar from Umar (رضي الله عنه) anchor this principle in both analogy and transmitted practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dhawi al-arham and their degree
Determine the exact relationships: e.g., paternal uncle's daughter vs. paternal aunt's son; both are children of cousins, thus same degree.
Degree here means the number of steps from the deceased (e.g., child of a cousin).
2
Check connection through an inheritor
For each relative, trace the lineage up to the deceased. If any ancestor (parent or grandparent) is a legal heir (asabah or fixed-share), that relative is connected through an inheritor.
Paternal uncle is an asabah; paternal aunt is only a dhu arham herself, not an inheritor.
3
Apply the rule
The dhu al-arham connected through an inheritor takes precedence over one not so connected, even if their degree is identical.
This is a wajib ruling.
4
Confirm exclusion of the other
The other relative, though of equal degree, is excluded entirely from inheritance due to this priority.
In the example: paternal uncle's daughter gets the entire estate (as dhu al-arham), paternal aunt's son gets nothing.

کی کی ای یو ای جان اور بھائی اور تاتاہوتو مامالونی نزدیک ناقد وانی اور بھانجا، بھاگی ہے۔
If the children of a father are equal in degree, then the first of them is the one who is closer to the deceased through an inheritor, and the closer is better than the farther. And the maternal grandfather is better than the child of a brother and the child of a sister. ... Examples: a paternal uncle's daughter and a paternal aunt's son: both are equal in degree, but the paternal uncle is an inheritor, so his daughter inherits, not the aunt's son. Similarly, a daughter's granddaughter and a son's granddaughter: the son's granddaughter inherits because he is an inheritor. Supporting athar from Umar: he placed the paternal aunt in the position of the father and the maternal aunt in the position of the mother.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage explains a key principle for when two dhawi al-arham are at the same degree of relationship: the one who is connected through a person who is a legal inheritor (i.e., an asabah or fixed-share heir) takes precedence over one who is connected solely through other dhawi al-arham. The examples clarify: a paternal uncle's daughter (the uncle is a residuary heir) inherits over a paternal aunt's son (the aunt is not an inheritor). Similarly, a son's granddaughter inherits over a daughter's granddaughter, because the son is an inheritor while the daughter is a fixed-share heir only. The athar (narration) from Umar (رضي الله عنه) is cited as evidence: he placed the paternal aunt in the position of the father and the maternal aunt in the position of the mother, indicating that connection through a closer inheritor gives priority.

Key Terms
ادلى بوارث (adla biwarith): Connected through an inheritor; a dhawi al-arham whose parent or grandparent is a legal heir of the deceased.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both inherit equally
B) Maternal aunt's son inherits all
C) Paternal aunt's daughter inherits all
D) Neither inherits

Key Takeaway
When two dhawi al-arham are equal in degree, the one whose connecting ancestor is a legal heir (asabah or fixed-share) is given priority in inheritance.

Maternal Grandfather's Priority Over Sibling's Descendants
States the priority rule among dhawi al-arham.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that the maternal grandfather (abu al-um) is preferred over the children of a brother or sister among dhawi al-arham and explain the reason based on proximity of degree.

Core Idea
Among dhawi al-arham, the nearer in degree inherits first.

In the Hanafi system of inheritance, when no fixed-share heirs (ashab al-furud) or residuary heirs (asabah) exist, inheritance passes to distant kindred (dhawi al-arham). This lesson presents a specific rule of priority among them: the maternal grandfather (father of the mother) is given precedence over the children of a sibling (brother's child or sister's child). This is because the maternal grandfather is one degree closer to the deceased than the sibling's children.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heirs involved
Recognize that in the given scenario, the deceased has no fixed-share or residuary heirs, so dhawi al-arham inherit. The potential heirs are: maternal grandfather (abu al-um), brother's child (son/daughter of a brother), and sister's child (son/daughter of a sister).
The maternal grandfather is a dhawi al-arham because he is not a fixed-share heir (he does not appear in the Quranic shares) nor a residuary heir (unless he is the paternal grandfather, which he is not).
2
Determine degrees of relation
Calculate the number of generational steps between each heir and the deceased. The maternal grandfather is two steps away: deceased → mother → maternal grandfather. The sibling's child is three steps away: deceased → parent (shared parent) → sibling → sibling's child. Thus the maternal grandfather is nearer by one degree.
In Hanafi faraid, degree counts only through the direct line; the maternal grandfather is connected via the mother.
3
Apply the principle of proximity
The general rule among dhawi al-arham is that the heir with the closer degree of relation to the deceased takes precedence over those with a more distant degree. This principle is based on the idea that blood relation strength diminishes with distance.
This is not explicitly stated in the Qur'an or Sunnah but is derived by analogy (qiyas) from the rules of asabah.
4
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, the maternal grandfather inherits to the exclusion of the sibling's children when these are the only heirs. If there are other dhawi al-arham with an equal degree, they would share proportionally.
This ruling is unanimous among Hanafi scholars and is part of the zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳۲۴۷) (۴) و ابو الام اولی من ولد الاخ والاخت. والا مقدم ہو گا بعیدی رشتہ والے سے۔
کی کی ای یو ای جان اور بھائی اور تاتاہوتو مامالونی نزدیک ناقد وانی اور بھانجا، بھاگی ہے۔
And the maternal grandfather is more entitled than the child of a brother and the child of a sister. ... [Corrupted Urdu text]
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage states a specific ruling within the chapter of dhawi al-arham: the maternal grandfather (father of the mother) is given priority over the descendants of a sibling (i.e., brother's child or sister's child). This is because the maternal grandfather is one degree closer to the deceased (being the mother's father) than siblings' children (who are two degrees away). The principle is that among dhawi al-arham, the nearer in degree takes precedence, and here the grandfather is nearer.

Key Terms
ابو الام (abu al-um): Maternal grandfather (father of the mother).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The son of a brother

D) Neither inherits

Key Takeaway
Among dhawi al-arham, the nearer in degree of relation takes precedence; the maternal grandfather excludes sibling's children.

Priority Among Dhawi al-Arham: Maternal Grandfather vs. Nephew/Niece
Explains priority rule with examples and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of priority among Dhawi al-Arham to determine which relative inherits, specifically distinguishing between the maternal grandfather and offspring of the brother/sister using the principle that the offspring of an heir takes precedence.

Core Idea
Who is closer to the heir: the one nearer by lineage or the one whose parent inherits? The offspring of an heir takes precedence.

This passage from the Hanafi faraid text explains the rule of priority among Dhawi al-Arham when no fixed-share or residuary heirs exist. The key principle is that the relative who is a descendant of a legal heir takes precedence over other Dhawi al-Arham of equal degree. The commentator illustrates this with two examples and then addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the maternal grandfather versus the children of a brother or sister.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle of priority
The passage begins with the rule 'The nearest to the heirs and the closest of them takes precedence over the farther.' This is the foundational axiom for ranking Dhawi al-Arham.
This principle is universal in Hanafi faraid for distant kindred.
2
Apply the rule to the maternal grandfather vs. children of brother/sister
The passage states that the maternal grandfather (abū al-umm) is more entitled than the children of the brother and sister. However, an intra-Hanafi dispute follows: Abu Hanifa argues the maternal grandfather is nearer in lineage, while the Sahibayn argue that the nephew/niece are direct offspring (sulbī) and thus better.
This is the core ikhtilaf of the lesson.
3
Trace the provided examples
The commentator gives two examples: (a) a cousin via paternal uncle (bint al-‘amm) vs. a cousin via paternal aunt (ibn al-khāl) – the former inherits because her father is an heir; (b) a granddaughter via son (bint al-ibn) vs. a granddaughter via daughter (bint al-bint) – the former inherits because her mother’s father (the son) is an heir. Both illustrate the principle that offspring of an heir take precedence.
These examples show consistent application.
4
Note the athar from Umar as a proof
The passage cites an athar (through Ziyad) where Umar placed the paternal aunt in the position of the father and the maternal aunt in the position of the mother. This supports the rule that the offspring of an heir are prioritized.
This is a dalil naqli used to justify the ruling.
5
Understand the mufta bihi position
Despite the Sahibayn's objection, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view: the maternal grandfather is preferred over the children of the brother and sister. This is based on closeness in lineage and the principle that the offspring of an heir rule still applies, but the maternal grandfather is himself closer.
The intra-Hanafi dispute is settled in favor of Abu Hanifa by later authorities.

Source Text

The nearest to the heirs and the closest of them takes precedence over the farther: (3247) The maternal grandfather is more entitled than the children of the brother and sister. ... The commentator explains with examples of cousin via paternal uncle vs. cousin via paternal aunt, and granddaughter via son vs. granddaughter via daughter, then cites an athar from Umar that the paternal aunt is treated as the father and the maternal aunt as the mother. Then rule (3247) repeated: The maternal grandfather takes precedence over the offspring of the brother and sister. Abu Hanifa says the maternal grandfather is nearer, while the Sahibayn say the nephew/niece (children of the brother/sister) take precedence because they are direct offspring.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and commentary on priority among Dhawi al-Arham. It first states the general principle that the closer relative takes precedence over the more distant. Then it specifies that the maternal grandfather (abū al-umm) is preferred over the children of the brother and sister (nephew/niece). The commentator provides two examples: first, a cousin via paternal uncle (bint al-‘amm) and a cousin via paternal aunt (ibn al-khāl): the former inherits because the paternal uncle is an heir, while the paternal aunt is not. Second, a granddaughter via son (ibt al-ibn) and a granddaughter via daughter (ibt al-bint): the former inherits because the son is an heir, while the daughter is not. The commentator then cites an athar from Umar (through Ziyad) that the paternal aunt is placed in the position of the father and the maternal aunt in the position of the mother, indicating that the offspring of an heir takes precedence. Then rule (3247) is repeated: the maternal grandfather is preferred over the offspring of the brother and sister. Here an intra-Hanafi dispute appears: Abu Hanifa holds that the maternal grandfather (who is closer by lineage) takes precedence over the nephew/niece, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the nephew/niece are direct descendants (nasab) and thus better than the maternal grandfather, who is only a maternal ascendant. The commentator concludes by stating the principle: direct offspring (sulbī) are better than Dhawi al-Arham.

Key Terms
ذوی الارحام (dhawī al-arhām): Relatives through the female line or through non-heir males; distant kindred who are not entitled to fixed shares or residuary.
وارث (wārith): A legal heir entitled to a share in inheritance.
عصبة (‘aṣabah): Residuary heirs who take the remainder after fixed shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the cousin via paternal uncle is female and females inherit in Hanafi law.
B) Because the paternal uncle is a legal heir, so his daughter is considered the offspring of an heir.
C) Because the cousin via paternal aunt is male and males are excluded in Dhawi al-Arham.
D) Because the paternal uncle is closer in degree than the paternal aunt.

Key Takeaway
Among Dhawi al-Arham, the descendant of a legal heir is preferred over one who is not, and the maternal grandfather takes precedence over a nephew/niece per the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

The Manumitter as Residuary Heir
Rule: manumitter takes surplus after fixed shares

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that the manumitter takes the surplus after fixed-share heirs and identify the hadith that establishes this right.

Core Idea
The manumitter inherits as an 'asabah through wala', taking the residue after fixed shares.

In Hanafi inheritance law, the manumitter (mu‘tiq) of a slave is not a blood relative, yet he has a right to inherit from the freed slave through the bond of clientage (wala'). This lesson explains that when a freed slave dies leaving fixed-share heirs but no other residuary heir, the manumitter takes the surplus estate. The proof is the Prophetic hadith, 'al-wala' belongs to the one who frees.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the scenario
Consider a freed slave who dies. Who are the heirs? The text specifies fixed-share heirs (dhawī al-sihām) and the manumitter as the only 'asabah.
The manumitter is like a stranger; his right comes only through wala'.
2
Apply the rule: manumitter takes the surplus
After the fixed-share heirs take their prescribed portions, the remaining estate (the surplus) goes to the manumitter, not back to the fixed-share heirs or to the state.
This treats the manumitter as an 'asabah, similar to a son or father.
3
Examine the dalil: hadith of Barira
The Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) said, 'al-wala' belongs to the one who frees.' This establishes that the manumitter has the right to inherit (through wala') from the freed slave.
The hadith is sahih and universally accepted.
4
Understand the legal basis (wajh al-dalalah)
The explicit phrase 'al-wala' belongs to ...' indicates ownership of the inheritance right; hence the manumitter becomes the residuary heir when no blood 'asabah exists.
The term 'al-wala' includes inheritance by clientage.
5
Distinguish from radd (return to fixed-share heirs)
In Hanafi law, the surplus after fixed shares normally returns to the fixed-share heirs (radd) if there is no 'asabah. However, the presence of the manumitter as an 'asabah prevents radd, giving the surplus to him instead.
This is an exception to the general rule of radd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۳۲۴۸ (۵) والـمـعـتـق احـق بـالـفـاضــل عن سهم ذوى السهام اذا لم تكن عصبة سواه. وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ آزاد کرنے والا زیادہ حقدار ہے بچے ہوئے مال کا ذوی الفروض سے جبکہ اس کے علاوہ کوئی عصبہ نہ ہو۔ آزاد شده غلام مرا۔ اس نے حصے والوں کو چھوڑا اور عصبہ کے طور پر آزاد کرنے والے آقا کو چھوڑا۔ آقا کے علاوہ کوئی قریب کا عصبہ نہیں تھا۔ ایسی صورت میں حصے والوں کے لینے کے بعد جو مال بچا وہ آزاد کرنے والے آقا کو ملے گا۔ یہ مال دوبارہ حصے والوں پر واپس نہیں لوٹایا جائے گا۔ حدیث میں ہے کہ ولاء یعنی آزاد شدہ غلام کی وراثت آقا کو ملے گی۔ عن عائشة قالت اشتـريـت بـريـرة فقال النبي الله اشتريها فان الولاء لمن اعتق (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب الولاء لمن احتق و میراث الملقیط ص ۹۹۹ نمبر (۲۷۵) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ آزاد کرنے والے کو ولاء ملے گی۔ اس لئے حصہ لینے والوں کے بعد کوئی عصبہ نہیں ہے تو آزاد کرنے والے آقا کو بقیہ مال بطور عصبہ ملیگا۔ هم ذوی السجام : حصے والے کا حصہ۔
(3248) The manumitter is more entitled to the surplus over the share of the fixed-share heirs when there is no other 'asabah. ... The commentator says: a freed slave dies leaving fixed-share heirs and the manumitter as the only 'asabah; then the manumitter takes the residue. This is based on the hadith of Barira: 'al-wala' belongs to the one who frees.'
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and its proof. Rule (3248) states that the manumitter (mu'tiq) of a slave is entitled to the surplus of the estate after the fixed-share heirs (ashab al-furud) have taken their shares, provided there is no other residuary heir ('asabah). The commentator explains the scenario: a freed slave dies, leaving only fixed-share heirs and the manumitter as the sole 'asabah. After the fixed-share heirs take their allocated portions, the remaining estate does not return to them but instead goes to the manumitter. The proof is the well-known hadith from Aisha (radiyallahu anha) about Barira, where the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) said, 'al-wala' belongs to the one who frees.' This establishes that the manumitter inherits through the bond of clientage (wala'), not through blood relation. Therefore, the manumitter is treated like an 'asabah in this context. The text clarifies that the residue does not revert to the fixed-share heirs.

Key Terms
معتق (mu‘tiq): The person who frees a slave; the manumitter.
ذوی السهام (dhawī al-sihām): Fixed-share heirs whose shares are specified in the Quran (e.g., spouse, parent, child).
ولاء (walā’): The bond of clientage between the manumitter and the freed slave, which gives the manumitter the right to inherit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, who is more entitled to the surplus after fixed-share heirs when there is no other 'asabah?
A) The state
B) The manumitter
C) The fixed-share heirs
D) The deceased's children

Key Takeaway
The manumitter inherits the residue after fixed shares as an 'asabah through the bond of wala', supported by explicit prophetic text.
