Inheritance of Mawla al-Muwalat
States that a client by contract inherits only when no blood heirs exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a mawla al-muwalat inherits according to Imam Abu Hanifa and cite the Quranic evidence for the precedence of blood relatives.

Core Idea
A contractual heir inherits only when no blood relative exists—even distant ones take precedence.

The institution of mawla al-muwalat (client by contract) allowed a convert without tribal ties to secure inheritance and blood-wit through a contract with a patron. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position on when such a client inherits, emphasizing that blood relationships—however distant—override the contractual bond. The passage cites Quranic verses to establish this hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the contract
Understand that mawla al-muwalat is created when a person embraces Islam at the hand of another and agrees that the patron inherits from him and pays his blood money.
This was a common arrangement in early Islam for those without family.
2
State the Hanafi ruling on inheritance
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the mawla al-muwalat inherits only when no other heir exists—no fixed-share heir (fard), no 'asabah, no manumitter (mawla al-'itaqah), and no Dhawi al-Arham (distant kindred).
The estate does not go to Bayt al-Mal in his presence.
3
Identify the conditions that prevent inheritance
List the heirs whose presence blocks the mawla: any blood relative, even a distant one, or the manumitter. The text emphasizes that even Dhawi al-Arham take precedence.
The Hanafi view gives contractual inheritance the lowest priority.
4
Locate the Quranic proof
The commentator cites Quran 8:75 (wa ulū al-arhām...) and implicitly 4:33 to show that blood relatives are nearer to each other than contractual ties.
The verse establishes a general principle: blood relations supersede contractual ones.
5
Compare with other schools
Note that the Shafi'i school considers mawla al-muwalat abrogated and holds that the estate goes to Bayt al-Mal when no blood heirs exist.
The difference stems from interpretation of whether the practice was abrogated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۳۲۴۹ (۶) ومولى الموالاة يرث. وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ ایک آدمی کسی آدمی کے ہاتھ پر اسلام لائے اور یوں کہے کہ آج سے آپ میرے وارث ہیں ۔ اگر میں مر گیا تو آپ میری وراثت لیں گے۔ اور اگر میں نے کوئی جنایت کی یا قتل کیا تو آپ میری دیت ادا کریں گے۔ اس کو مولی موالات کہتے ہیں ۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک کوئی اور وارث نہ ہو تو مولی موالات کو وراثت ملے گی۔ مال بیت المال میں داخل نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ اگر حصے دار موجود ہو یا عصبہ ہو یا مولی عقاقہ موجود ہوتب تو مولی موالات کو نہیں ملے گا۔ اور یہ لوگ نہ ہوں تب مولی موالات کو ملے گا۔ آیت میں ہے واولوا الارحام بعضهم اولى ببعض في كتاب الله ( آیت ۷۵، سورۃ الانفال ۸) اس آیت میں ہے ذوی الارحام بعض بعض سے بہتر ہے کا حکم ہے۔ اس لئے ذوی الارحام بھی موجود ہو تو مولی موالات کو نہیں ملے گا۔ کیونکہ یہ لوگ نسبی طور پر وارث ہیں۔ اور مولی موالات عقد کرنے کی وجہ سے وارث ہیں (۲) ذوی الارحام نہ ہوں تو مولی موالات کو وراثت ملے گی اس کی دلیل یہ آیت ہے۔ ولکل
(3249) And the client by contract (mawla al-muwalat) inherits. ... The commentator explains: a person embraces Islam at the hand of another and says 'from today you are my heir; if I die you inherit from me, and if I commit a crime you pay my blood money.' This is mawla al-muwalat. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if no other heir exists, this client inherits; the estate does not go to Bayt al-Mal. But if a fixed-share heir, 'asabah, or manumitter (mawla al-'itaqah) exists, mawla al-muwalat does not inherit. The verse 'wa ulū al-arhām...' shows that even Dhawi al-Arham take precedence over mawla al-muwalat because they are blood relatives.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and its commentary on the inheritance of a client by contract (mawla al-muwalat). This is a pre-Islamic practice continued in Islam: a person who has no tribal ties can enter a contractual relationship with another, whereby the latter agrees to inherit from the former and pay his blood money. The ruling states that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, this client inherits only when there is no other heir at all—no fixed-share heir, no 'asabah, no manumitter (mawla al-'itaqah), and not even any Dhawi al-Arham (distant kindred). The commentator cites Quran 8:75: 'And those of kinship are nearer to one another in the Book of Allah' to prove that even Dhawi al-Arham take precedence over mawla al-muwalat. The reason is that mawla al-muwalat is only a contractual heir, while blood relatives are natural heirs. Therefore, the estate of a person who has any blood relative, however distant, does not go to the mawla al-muwalat. Only when there is absolutely no blood relative does he inherit, and the estate does not go to the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal). The text ends with 'wa li kull' which might be the beginning of 'wa li kullin ja'alna mawaliya' (Quran 4:33), another proof.

Key Terms
مولی الموالات (mawlā al-muwālāt): A client by contract: a person who enters into an inheritance and blood-wit contract with another, typically upon conversion to Islam.
مولی عتاقه (mawlā al-‘itāqah): The manumitter (mu'tiq) who has the right of inheritance through wala'; the client by emancipation.
بیت المال (bayt al-māl): The public treasury of the Muslim state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Only when there is no fixed-share heir or 'asabah
C) Only when there are no blood relatives at all, including Dhawi al-Arham
D) Only when the deceased has no children

Key Takeaway
A contractual heir (mawla al-muwalat) inherits only when no blood relative exists—Quranic evidence proves blood ties take absolute precedence.

The Manumitter's Right to Surplus Inheritance
A ruling on the manumitter's inheritance via wala'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the manumitter takes the surplus of a freed slave's estate when no blood residuary heir exists after fixed-share holders.

Core Idea
The manumitter is an asabah for the freed slave's estate when no blood residuary heir exists.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling in inheritance by wala' (clientship). When a freed slave dies, leaving fixed-share heirs (like a spouse or parents) but no blood residuary heir from his own relatives, the surplus after distributing the fixed shares goes to the manumitter. This right is established by a clear hadith and is unanimously accepted within the Hanafi school. Understanding this rule prevents confusion with other categories like dhawi al-arham or radd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Consider a freed slave who passes away, leaving behind fixed-share heirs (dhawi al-siham) and the manumitter, but no other residuary heir (asabah) from among his own blood relatives.
The fixed-share heirs have already received their prescribed portions.
2
State the ruling
The manumitter (mu'tiq) is entitled to the surplus (fadil) of the estate. He takes it as an asabah (residuary heir). The surplus is not returned to the fixed-share heirs (radd) nor given to distant relatives (dhawi al-arham).
This is a special right of wala' that overrides other claims.
3
Locate the proof
The primary evidence is the hadith from Aisha (RA) in Sahih al-Bukhari: 'al-wala' li-man a'taqa' (wala' belongs to the one who emancipates).
This hadith is ahad (single-narrator) but its meaning is clear and acted upon.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'al-wala' li-man a'taqa' is a general attribution of wala' — including inheritance rights — to the manumitter. The imperative 'ishtariha' (buy her) in the hadith context is permissive, but the attribution is a legal statement establishing the manumitter's entitlement as a residuary heir.
Hanafi usul treats this as a definitive indication (dalala qati'a) even though the transmission is zanni.
5
Note the practical consequence
In application, after fixed-share heirs take their shares, the manumitter takes the remainder. This prevents the estate from being redistributed among fixed-share heirs (no radd) and excludes dhawi al-arham entirely.
The manumitter's right is stronger than that of distant relatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتق أحق بالفاضل عن سهم ذوي السهام إذا لم تكن عصبة سواه. وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ آزاد کرنے والا زیادہ حقدار ہے بچے ہوئے مال کا ذوی الفروض سے جبکہ اس کے علاوہ کوئی عصبہ نہ ہو۔ آزاد شده غلام مرا۔ اس نے حصے والوں کو چھوڑا اور عصبہ کے طور پر آزاد کرنے والے آقا کو چھوڑا۔ آقا کے علاوہ کوئی قریب کا عصبہ نہیں تھا۔ ایسی صورت میں حصے والوں کے لینے کے بعد جو مال بچا وہ آزاد کرنے والے آقا کو ملے گا۔ یہ مال دوبارہ حصے والوں پر واپس نہیں لوٹایا جائے گا۔ حدیث میں ہے کہ ولاء یعنی آزاد شدہ غلام کی وراثت آقا کو ملے گی۔ عن عائشة قالت اشتريت بريرة فقال النبي اشتريها فان الولاء لمن اعتق (بخاری شریف، باب الولاء لمن اعتق) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ آزاد کرنے والے کو ولاء ملے گی۔ اس لئے حصہ لینے والوں کے بعد کوئی عصبہ نہیں ہے تو آزاد کرنے والے آقا کو بقیہ مال بطور عصبہ ملے گا۔ هم ذوی السهام : حصے والے کا حصہ۔
The manumitter is more entitled to the surplus over the share of the fixed-share holders when there is no other residuary heir besides him. And he says: The manumitter is more entitled to the surplus of the property from the fixed-share holders when there is no other residuary heir besides him. A freed slave died. He left behind fixed-share holders and left the manumitter as a residuary heir. There was no other close residuary heir besides the master. In such a case, after the fixed-share holders take their shares, the remaining property goes to the manumitter. This property is not returned to the fixed-share holders. The hadith states that wala', i.e., the inheritance of the freed slave, goes to the master. From Aisha (RA) who said: I bought Barirah, and the Prophet (SAW) said: Buy her, for wala' belongs to the one who emancipates (Bukhari). This hadith indicates that the manumitter gets wala'. Therefore, after the fixed-share holders, if there is no residuary heir, the remaining property goes to the manumitter as a residuary heir.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This is a ruling on inheritance by wala' (clientship). When a freed slave dies and leaves fixed-share heirs (like a spouse or parents with prescribed shares) but no blood residuary heir (asabah), the surplus after distributing the fixed shares goes to the manumitter (mu'tiq). This is because the hadith from Aisha (RA) explicitly states that wala' belongs to the one who emancipates. The manumitter is treated as an asabah in this context, taking the residue. The surplus is not returned to the fixed-share heirs (radd) nor given to dhawi al-arham, as the wala' right is stronger. This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is agreed upon among the madhhab.

Key Terms
المعتق (al-mu'tiq): The person who emancipates a slave and thereby acquires the right of wala' (inheritance from the freed slave).
الفاضل (al-fadil): The surplus or residue of the estate after fixed shares are distributed.
ذوي السهام (dhawi al-siham): Heirs who receive fixed Quranic shares (like 1/2, 1/4, 1/8, etc.).
عصبة (asabah): A residuary heir who takes the remaining estate after fixed shares, or the whole estate if no fixed-share heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Dhawi al-arham
B) The manumitter
C) The public treasury
D) It is redistributed among fixed-share heirs

Key Takeaway
The manumitter takes the surplus of a freed slave's estate as an asabah when no blood residuary heir exists.

Inheritance by Mawla al-Muwalat: The Hanafi Position and Proofs
States the ruling on mawla al-muwalat inheritance with proofs and dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on inheritance by mawla al-muwalat, identify the conditions under which it applies, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument regarding abrogation.

Core Idea
Mawla al-muwalat inherits when no blood relative or manumitter exists; contractual patronage is a valid cause of inheritance in Hanafi law.

This lesson examines the institution of mawla al-muwalat — a contract of mutual inheritance and liability. You will learn when this contractual heir inherits, what proofs support the Hanafi view, and how the Shafi'i school differs. Understanding this ruling clarifies the priority of blood ties over contractual bonds in Islamic inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Define mawla al-muwalat
Explain the contract: a new Muslim makes an agreement with his mentor that the mentor inherits and bears blood-money.
This contract is called muwalat.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The mawla inherits only when no fixed-share heir, residuary, mawla al-ataqah, or dhawi al-arham exist.
Absence of all blood relatives is key.
3
Identify the Hanafi proofs
List the evidence: Quran 4:33, hadith of Tamim al-Dari, practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab.
Each proof supports contractual inheritance.
4
Present the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i claims that Quran 4:33 is abrogated by Quran 8:75, supported by Hasan al-Basri's practice.
This would make the estate go to Bayt al-Mal.
5
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response argues that 8:75 applies to blood relatives, not contractual relationships; hence no abrogation.
The Hanafi view is considered stronger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومولى الموالاة يرث۔ ایک آدمی کسی آدمی کے ہاتھ پر اسلام لائے اور یوں کہے کہ آج سے آپ میرے وارث ہیں ۔ اگر میں مر گیا تو آپ میری وراثت لیں گے۔ اور اگر میں نے کوئی جنایت کی یا قتل کیا تو آپ میری دیت ادا کریں گے۔ اس کو مولی موالات کہتے ہیں ۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک کوئی اور وارث نہ ہو تو مولی موالات کو وراثت ملے گی۔ مال بیت المال میں داخل نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ اگر حصے دار موجود ہو یا عصبہ ہو یا مولی عقاقہ موجود ہوتب تو مولی موالات کو نہیں ملے گا۔ اور یہ لوگ نہ ہوں تب مولی موالات کو ملے گا۔ آیت میں ہے واولوا الارحام بعضهم اولى ببعض في كتاب الله ( آیت ۷۵، سورۃ الانفال ۸) اس آیت میں ہے ذوی الارحام بعض بعض سے بہتر ہے کا حکم ہے۔ اس لئے ذوی الارحام بھی موجود ہو تو مولی موالات کو نہیں ملے گا۔ کیونکہ یہ لوگ نسبی طور پر وارث ہیں۔ اور مولی موالات عقد کرنے کی وجہ سے وارث ہیں (۲) ذوی الارحام نہ ہوں تو مولی موالات کو وراثت ملے گی اس کی دلیل یہ آیت ہے۔ ولکل ام م م [؟] (آیت ۳۳، سورۃ النساء ۴) اس آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ جس سے عہد و پیمان کیا اس کو اس کا حصہ دو یعنی وارث نہ ہونے پر وہ وارث ہوگا (۳) حدیث میں بھی اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن تميم الدارى انه قال يا رسول الله ! ما السنة فى الرجل يسلم على يدى الرجل من المسلمين ؟ قال هو أولى الناس بمحياه و مماته (ابوداؤد شریف، باب الرجل يسلم علی یدی الرجل) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ مولی موالات زندگی اور موت میں زیادہ بہتر ہے یعنی آخیر میں اس کو وراثت ملے گی (۴) اثر میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ ان عمر بن الخطاب قال اذا والى رجل رجلا فله ميراثه وعليه عقله (مصنف ابن ابی شیبة) اس اثر میں ہے کہ کسی سے موالات کیا تو وہ اس کا وارث ہوگا اور دیت بھی دے گا۔ امام شافعی مولی موالات کو وراثت نہیں دیتے ہیں۔ وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اوپر کی آیت۔ اولوا الارحام بعضهم اولى ببعض (آیت ۷۵ سورۃ الانفال ۸) کی وجہ سے آیت۔ والذين عقدت ايمانكم فأتوهم نصيبهم (آیت ۳۳ ، سورۃ النساء ۴) منسوخ ہے۔ اس لئے حصے دار ذوی الارحام اور مولی عقاقہ نہ بھی ہو تب بھی مولی موالات کو نہیں ملے گا ۔ بلکہ مال بیت المال میں داخل کر دیا جائے گا (۲) اثر میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن الحسن قال ميراثه للمسلمين وعقله عليهم (مصنف ابن ابی شیبة) اس اثر میں ہے کہ اس کی وراثت عام مسلمانوں کو ملے گی یعنی بیت المال میں داخل ہوگی۔
And the client by agreement inherits. A person embraces Islam at the hands of another and says: from today you are my heir; if I die, you inherit from me; and if I commit a crime or kill, you pay my blood-money. This is called mawla al-muwalat. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when there is no other heir, the mawla al-muwalat inherits. The property is not put into the public treasury. If there is a fixed-share heir, or a residuary heir, or a mawla al-ataqah (manumitter), then the mawla al-muwalat does not inherit. If these are absent, then the mawla al-muwalat inherits. The verse says: 'But those of kinship are more deserving one of another in the decree of Allah' (8:75). This verse indicates that dhawi al-arham are better than each other. Therefore, if dhawi al-arham are present, the mawla al-muwalat does not inherit because they are relatives by lineage, while the mawla al-muwalat inherits by contract. (2) If dhawi al-arham are absent, then the mawla al-muwalat inherits. The evidence for this is the verse: 'And for everyone We have made allies [mawali] of what parents and near relatives leave; and those whom your right hands have made a covenant, give them their share' (4:33). From this verse it is understood that one who makes a covenant should be given his share, meaning that when there is no heir, he becomes an heir. (3) The hadith also proves this: From Tamim al-Dari that he said: O Messenger of Allah, what is the Sunnah regarding a man who embraces Islam at the hands of another Muslim? He said: He is the most entitled to him in his life and death (Abu Dawud). This hadith indicates that the mawla al-muwalat is most entitled in life and death, meaning in the end he inherits. (4) The practice also proves it: Umar ibn al-Khattab said: If a man makes a clientage agreement with another, then he inherits from him and bears his blood-money (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah). This practice indicates that whoever makes a clientage agreement inherits and pays blood-money. Imam al-Shafi'i does not give inheritance to the mawla al-muwalat. He says that because of the verse 'But those of kinship are more deserving' (8:75), the verse 'And those whom your right hands have made a covenant, give them their share' (4:33) is abrogated. Therefore, even if there is no fixed-share heir, no residuary heir, and no mawla al-ataqah, the mawla al-muwalat still does not inherit; rather, the property is put into the public treasury. (2) The practice also proves this: From Hasan that he said: His inheritance is for the Muslims, and his blood-money is upon them (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah). This practice indicates that his inheritance goes to the general Muslims, i.e., it enters the public treasury.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on mawla al-muwalat (client by agreement) and includes a full inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. According to Abu Hanifa, if a person embraces Islam at the hands of another and makes a formal agreement that the latter will inherit from him and pay his blood-money, then this contract (muwalat) is valid and the mawla inherits when there are no blood relatives (including dhawi al-arham) and no manumitter (mawla al-ataqah). The proofs include: Quran 4:33, the hadith of Tamim al-Dari, and the practice of Umar. Imam al-Shafi'i rejects this, arguing that Quran 4:33 is abrogated by Quran 8:75, and that the estate should go to the public treasury. The Hanafi response is that 8:75 refers to blood relations, not contractual relationships, so there is no abrogation. The Hanafi position is the relied-upon one.

Key Terms
مولى الموالاة (mawla al-muwalat): A person who enters into a contract of mutual inheritance and liability (muwalat) with another, typically after embracing Islam at the other's hands.
مولى العتاقة (mawla al-ataqah): The manumitter who has the right of wala' from emancipation; this person takes priority over mawla al-muwalat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for mawla al-muwalat to inherit according to Abu Hanifa?
A) No fixed-share heir
B) No residuary heir
C) No mawla al-ataqah
D) The mawla must be a non-Muslim

Key Takeaway
Mawla al-muwalat inherits only in the absence of any blood relative or manumitter, based on Quran, hadith, and the practice of Umar, despite the Shafi'i claim of abrogation.

Inheritance by Wala': Dispute Over the Master's Father and Son
States the Hanafi disagreement on distribution among the master's father and son.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the distribution of inheritance by wala' when the master's father and son survive, and explain the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
The son excludes the father in wala' inheritance, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; Abu Yusuf gives the father a fixed sixth.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning inheritance through wala' (clientage). When a freed slave dies without blood heirs and his master is also deceased, the master's father and son both claim the estate. The Hanafi masters differ on whether the father receives a share. Understanding this dispute clarifies the principles of priority among asabah (residuary heirs) and the role of analogy in inheritance law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heirs and the basis of their claim
The master's father and son both claim inheritance through wala', as the freed slave has no blood relatives and the master is dead. Determine their potential entitlement as residuaries.
Wala' is established by manumission; the master and his agnatic heirs inherit from the freed slave.
2
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
According to both of them ('indahuma), the son takes the entire estate; the father receives nothing.
They analogize to blood inheritance where the son, as a closer asabah, excludes the father.
3
State the ruling of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf gives the father a fixed 1/6 and the son the remaining 5/6.
He analogizes the father's share to the 1/6 he receives in blood inheritance when a son exists.
4
Compare the two analogies
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad apply the asabah priority strictly: the primary residuary (son) excludes all others. Abu Yusuf treats the father as having a fixed share similar to the Quranic share.
The difference hinges on whether wala' inheritance follows the same rules of priority as blood inheritance or incorporates fixed shares.
5
Note the evidence from Zayd ibn Thabit
The text cites Zayd ibn Thabit's practice that 'the wealth is for the son, and nothing for the father', which supports the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
This companion practice strengthens the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا ترك المعتق اب مولاه وابن مولاه فماله للابن عندهما وقال ابو يوسف للابن خمسة اسداس وللاب سدس. آزاد شدہ غلام مرا۔ اس کا کوئی نسبی وارث نہیں تھا، آقا بھی زندہ نہیں تھا بلکہ آقا کا باپ اور بیٹا تھا تو امام ابوحنیفہ اور امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ باپ کو نہیں ملے گا۔ سب مال بیٹے کومل جائے گا۔ آزاد شدہ غلام کا مال عصبہ کے طور پر ملتا ہے اور وارثین میں بھی عصبہ کے طور پر تقسیم ہوتا ہے۔ اور بیٹا پہلا عصبہ ہے اس کے بعد باپ کا نمبر ہے۔ اس لئے بیٹے کی موجودگی میں باپ کو کچھ نہیں ملے گا (۲) اثر میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ وقال زيد بن ثابت المال للابن وليس للاب شئ.
And if the freed slave leaves the father of his master and the son of his master, then his wealth belongs to the son according to both of them (Abu Hanifah and Muhammad), and Abu Yusuf said: for the son five-sixths and for the father one-sixth. A freed slave died. He had no blood relative, and his master was also dead, but the master's father and son were alive. Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad say that the father will not receive anything; all the wealth will go to the son. The property of the freed slave is taken as a residuary (asabah) and is distributed among heirs as a residuary. The son is the first residuary, and after him the father's turn comes. Therefore, in the presence of the son, the father receives nothing. (2) Evidence for this is the practice: Zayd ibn Thabit said: The wealth is for the son, and there is nothing for the father.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding inheritance by wala' when the manumitter (master) is dead and the freed slave leaves the master's father and son. Both the father and son are potential heirs through wala', but they differ in priority. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad analogize this to blood inheritance, where the son (as a closer asabah) excludes the father entirely. Thus, the son takes the entire estate. Abu Yusuf, however, treats the father as having a fixed share (1/6, analogous to the father's share in blood inheritance when a son exists) and gives the son the residue. The text also cites the practice of Zayd ibn Thabit supporting the view that the son takes all. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it aligns with the principle that the son is a stronger residuary.

Key Terms
المعتق (al-mu'taq): The freed slave (the deceased).
ابن مولاه (ibn mawlahu): The son of the master (the manumitter), i.e., the manumitter's son.
عصبة (asabah): Residuary heir; one who inherits the remainder after fixed shares, or takes all if no fixed shares exist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) the father
B) the son
C) the father gets 1/6, the son gets 5/6
D) equally divided

Key Takeaway
In inheritance by wala', the son as the closer residuary excludes the father entirely, following the dominant Hanafi view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, supported by the practice of Zayd ibn Thabit.

Inheritance of a Freed Slave: Patron's Father vs Son
Intra-Hanafi dispute on distribution of mawla's estate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the distribution of a freed slave's estate when the patron's son and father survive, and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
The patron's son inherits the entire estate as the nearest 'asaba; the father is excluded.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on how the estate of a freed slave (mawla al-muwalat) is distributed when the patron's father and son survive. The dominant Hanafi position holds that the son takes all, while Abu Yusuf grants the father one-sixth. Understanding this dispute clarifies the operation of 'asaba priority in inheritance law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
The freed slave (mawla) dies leaving no blood relatives. His patron is also deceased, but the patron's father and son survive. Determine which 'asaba categories they belong to.
The son is a primary 'asaba; the father is a secondary 'asaba (included when no son exists).
2
Apply the 'asaba priority rule
In Hanafi faraid, 'asaba inherit in order: son, then grandson, then father, then grandfather, etc. The son, being higher in rank, excludes the father entirely from the residue.
This is the standard 'asaba hierarchy.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad apply the standard 'asaba rule: son takes all. Abu Yusuf analogizes to the regular inheritance where a father receives 1/6 when a son exists, but here the estate is acquired via patron's 'asaba rights, not blood. Abu Yusuf's view is based on treating the father as a fixed-share heir (fard) rather than residue.
Abu Yusuf's position is a minority within the school.
4
Evaluate the proofs
Both sides rely on proofs. The dominant Hanafi view uses the athar of Zaid ibn Thabit who gave the entire estate to the son. Abu Yusuf might argue from general analogy with blood inheritance. Also consider the Shafi'i objection that mawla inheritance is abrogated entirely.
The Hanafi response refutes abrogation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the son inherits everything. This is consistent with the general 'asaba rule and is supported by the majority of Hanafi authorities.
Al-Marghinani and others adopt this view.

(3250) (7) If the freed slave leaves the father of his patron and the son of his patron, then his property is for the son according to both (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). And He said: 'We have made you patrons of what the parents and relatives leave, and those with whom you have made a covenant, give them their share' (Surah an-Nisa 4:33). From this verse it is understood that one with whom a covenant is made should be given his share, meaning if there is no heir, he becomes an heir. (3) There is also proof in hadith: from Tamim ad-Dari that he said, 'O Messenger of Allah, what is the Sunnah concerning a man who accepts Islam at the hands of a man from the Muslims?' He said: 'He is the most entitled of people to his life and his death.' (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi). From this hadith it is understood that the mawla muwalat is more entitled in life and death, meaning ultimately he will receive inheritance. (4) There is proof in athar: Ibn Umar ibn al-Khattab said, 'If a man makes a covenant of clientage (muwalat) with another man, then he has his inheritance and bears his blood money' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah). In this athar, if someone makes muwalat, then he will be his heir and will also pay the blood money. Imam Shafi'i does not give inheritance to mawla muwalat. He says that due to the verse 'Those related by blood are more entitled to one another' (Surah al-Anfal 8:75), the verse 'And those with whom you have made a covenant, give them their share' (Surah an-Nisa 4:33) is abrogated. Therefore, even if there are no heirs among dhawi al-arham and no 'asaba, the mawla muwalat does not receive anything; rather, the wealth is placed in the public treasury. (2) There is proof in athar: from al-Hasan that he said, 'His inheritance is for the Muslims and his blood money is upon them' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah). In this athar, his inheritance goes to the general Muslims, i.e., it enters the treasury. (3250) (7) If the freed slave leaves the father of his patron and his son, then his property is for the son according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad. Imam Abu Yusuf said: one sixth for the father and the remainder for the son. The freed slave died. He had no blood relatives. The patron was not alive, but the patron's father and son were there. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say: the father does not receive anything; all property goes to the son. The property of a freed slave is acquired as 'asaba, and it is distributed among heirs also as 'asaba. The son is the first 'asaba, after him comes the father. Therefore, in the presence of the son, the father gets nothing. (2) There is proof in athar: Zaid ibn Thabit said, 'The property is for the son, and there is nothing for the father.' Footnote: (a) 'We have made you patrons...' (b) Tamim ad-Dari said: 'What is the Sunnah...' (c) Umar said: 'If a man makes muwalat...' (d) 'Those related by blood are more entitled...' (e) 'And those with whom you have made a covenant...' (f) Al-Hasan said: 'His inheritance is for the Muslims...' Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said: for the father one sixth and the remainder for the son.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Dhawi al-Arham presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the inheritance of a freed slave (mawla al-muwalat) when his patron's father and son survive. The author first establishes the general legitimacy of mawla al-muwalat inheritance through the Quranic verse (4:33), hadith of Tamim ad-Dari, and athar of Umar. Then he reports Imam Shafi'i's objection that the verse is abrogated by 8:75, so the estate should go to the public treasury.

Then the specific case: the freed slave dies with no blood heirs, but his patron's father and son survive. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the entire estate goes to the son, because the patron's son is the nearest 'asaba. The father is excluded because he is a lower rank of 'asaba. Abu Yusuf disagrees, granting the father one-sixth and the son the rest, analogizing to regular inheritance where a father receives one-sixth in the presence of a son. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rely on the principle that the freed slave's estate is acquired by 'asaba rights, and the son is the primary 'asaba. They also cite the athar of Zaid ibn Thabit who gave everything to the son. The reliance (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, consistent with the general Hanafi rule that the patron's son takes all as 'asaba.

Key Terms
مولی الموالات (mawla al-muwalat): A person who becomes a client through a covenant of mutual inheritance and support, not through emancipation.
عصبة ('asabah): Male agnatic relatives who inherit the residue after Quranic shares, or the entire estate if no Quranic heir.
ذوی الارحام (dhawi al-arham): Relatives through females or other non-agnatic connections; in Hanafi law, they inherit only in the absence of 'asaba and Quranic heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a freed slave (mawla al-muwalat) who leaves the patron's father and son, what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) The father and son split the estate equally.
B) The son receives the entire estate.
C) The father receives one-sixth and the son the remainder.
D) The estate goes to the public treasury.

Key Takeaway
The patron's son, as the nearest 'asaba, inherits the entire estate of a freed slave with no blood heirs, excluding the patron's father according to the majority Hanafi position.

Mawla Inheritance: Patron's Grandfather vs Brother
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mawla inheritance with patron's grandfather and brother

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on inheritance of a freed slave when the patron's grandfather and brother survive, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute and the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
The grandfather excludes the brother in mawla inheritance according to Abu Hanifa, as clientage follows lineage.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the inheritance of a freed slave (mawla al-muwalat) when the patron's grandfather and brother both survive. The three founding imams of the Hanafi school – Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani – hold differing views. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace how lineage (nasab) and clientage (wala) interact in inheritance law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Abu Hanifa rules for the estate of a freed slave when the patron's grandfather and brother survive.
Abu Hanifa: grandfather takes the entire estate.
2
Identify the opponent's view
State the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn). Note that multiple opinions are attributed to them.
Sahibayn: both are equal (half each) or one-third/two-thirds.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Explain the reasoning of Sahibayn: both grandfather and brother are 'asaba (agnatic heirs), so they should share, with the male receiving double the female's share.
Their analogy: since both are 'asaba, they share as in regular inheritance between two males.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Present Abu Hanifa's proof – the statement of az-Zuhri that clientage belongs to the grandfather because lineage is attributed to him, not the brother.
The grandfather draws the clientage just as a son does; he is the nearest relative.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence of the dispute: under Abu Hanifa the brother gets nothing; under Sahibayn he gets a share.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ ۳۲۵۱ (۸) فان ترک جد مولاه واخا مولاه فـالـمـال لـلـجد عند ابي حنيفة رحمه الله وقال ابو يوسف و محمد هما سواء (أي بين الجد والاخ) وقال أبو يوسف: الثلث للجد والثلثان للاخ وقيل: الثلث للاخ والثلثان للجد. والاظهر ان المال بينهما نصفين. دليل ابي حنيفة قول الزهري: الولاء للجد لانه ينسب الى الجد ولا ينسب الى الاخ. ووجهه ان الجد يستجر الولاء كما يستجره الابن. وقال ابو يوسف ومحمد: هما سواء لان كلاهما عصبة. فان المال بينهما للذكر مثل حظ الانثيين. والصحيح ما قاله ابو حنيفة لان الجد اولى من الاخ لانه اسبق نسبا. والله اعلم. حاشیة: (الف) دوسری روایت میں ہے۔ عن الحسن قال هو للابن (ب) (مصنف ابن ابی شبیه ۱۰ ارجل مات و ترک ابنه واباه و مولاه ، ثم مات المولی و ترک مالا ، ج سادس ، ص ۲۹۴ نمبر۳۱۵۱۳/۳۱۵۱۱) اس اثر میں ہے کہ وراثت بیٹے کو دی جائے گی۔
(3251) (8) If he leaves the grandfather of his patron and the brother of his patron, then the property is for the grandfather according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: both are equal. Abu Yusuf said: one third for the grandfather and two thirds for the brother, and it is said: one third for the brother and two thirds for the grandfather. And the more apparent is that the property is half between them. The proof of Abu Hanifa is the statement of az-Zuhri: 'The clientage (wala) is for the grandfather, because he is attributed to the grandfather, not to the brother.' And its reasoning is that the grandfather draws the clientage as the son draws it. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: both are equal because both are 'asaba. So the property is between them as for a male the share of two females. And the correct is what Abu Hanifa said because the grandfather is more entitled than the brother because he is earlier in lineage. And Allah knows best. Footnote: (a) In another narration: from al-Hasan that he said: 'It is for the son' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah). In this athar, the inheritance is given to the son.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents another intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the inheritance of a freed slave (mawla al-muwalat) when the patron's grandfather and brother are the claimants. In the original matn, the author states that according to Abu Hanifa, the entire estate goes to the grandfather. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad initially appear to say 'both are equal,' but then different opinions are attributed: Abu Yusuf gives one-third to grandfather and two-thirds to brother, and another report gives one-third to brother and two-thirds to grandfather. The author then remarks that the most apparent view is that the estate is split equally.

The proof for Abu Hanifa is the athar of az-Zuhri, who reasoned that 'wala' (the right of inheritance through clientage) is attached to the grandfather because he is the one to whom the patron is attributed, not to the brother. The grandfather draws the clientage similarly to how the son draws it. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) argue that both are 'asaba (agnatic heirs) and thus should share, with the male receiving twice the female's share (though here both are male, so equal shares).

The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa: the grandfather excludes the brother entirely. This is because the grandfather is a closer relative to the patron than the brother, and the right of clientage follows the nearest relative of the patron after his death.

Key Terms
جد (jadd): Paternal grandfather of the patron, an 'asaba heir in inheritance.
اخ (akh): Patron's full or consanguine brother, also an 'asaba but lower in rank than grandfather.
ولاء (wala): The right of inheritance through clientage (muwalat or emancipation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The grandfather and the brother share equally.
B) The grandfather receives the entire estate.
C) The brother receives the entire estate.
D) The estate is divided one-third to grandfather, two-thirds to brother.

Key Takeaway
In mawla al-muwalat inheritance, the patron's grandfather excludes the brother entirely, as clientage follows the nearest relative in lineage according to Abu Hanifa.

Inheritance of Wala' Among Patron's Father and Son
Ruling with evidence from Shuraih on wala' distribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wala' inheritance between the patron's father and son, identify the dalil from Shuraih, and distinguish Abu Yusuf's position from the minority narration.

Core Idea
In wala' inheritance, the patron's father takes one-sixth and the son takes the remainder, following Shuraih's precedent.

This passage presents a specific ruling on the inheritance of wala'—the right of a patron to inherit from a freed slave. Imam Abu Yusuf establishes a fixed share for the patron's father, while giving the rest to the son. The evidence comes directly from the companion Shuraih, whose allocation is recorded. However, there is an intra-Hanafi disagreement, as another narration gives the entire wala' to the son. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Proponent
State Abu Yusuf's ruling: the patron's father receives one-sixth of the freed slave's estate, and the son receives the remainder.
2
Locate the Dalil
The evidence is the practice of the companion Shuraih, narrated by Qatada from Shuraih and Zayd ibn Thabit.
The chain is: Qatada → Shuraih and Zayd ibn Thabit.
3
Analyze the Wajh al-Dalalah
Shuraih explicitly allocated one-sixth to the father and the rest to the son, providing direct proof for the ruling.
The ruling is based on a qawl sahabi, which is a valid but zanni dalil.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Disagreement
The narration from al-Hasan (and possibly Zayd ibn Thabit) states that the entire wala' belongs to the son, excluding the father.
This is a minority view within the Hanafi school.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Yusuf's, as it is supported by stronger evidence from Shuraih and consistent with the general principles of wala' inheritance.
The tarjih is based on the strength of the evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ذوى الارحام ابویوسف رحمه الله تعالى للاب السدس والباقى للابن للاب شيء عن الحسن قال هو للابن عن قتادة عن شريح و زيد بن ثابت في رجل مات وترك ابنه واباه ومولاه ثم مات المولى وترك مالا فقال شريح لابيه السدس وما بقى فللابن
Chapter of Dhawi al-Arham. Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said: For the father one-sixth, and the remainder for the son. The father gets nothing. From al-Hasan: he said it is for the son. From Qatada from Shuraih and Zayd ibn Thabit concerning a man who died and left his son, his father, and his freed slave, then the freed slave died and left property: Shuraih said: For the father one-sixth, and what remains is for the son.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on how wala' (patronage) inheritance is distributed among the patron's descendants when the freed slave dies without heirs. The ruling is attributed to Imam Abu Yusuf: the patron's father receives one-sixth, and the patron's son receives the remainder. The phrase 'للاب شيء' is interpreted by the commentator as meaning the father gets nothing (i.e., one-sixth is a small share, but this reading is contested). The narration from al-Hasan states that the entire wala' belongs to the son, excluding the father. The evidence provided is the practice of the companion Shuraih, who explicitly gave the father one-sixth and the son the rest. This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school and shows a disagreement among early authorities on the extent of the father's share in wala' inheritance.

Key Terms
ذوي الارحام (Dhawi al-Arham): Uterine relatives; those related through females, who inherit in the absence of agnatic heirs according to Hanafi law.
ولاء (Wala'): The right of inheritance a patron (master) has over the estate of a freed slave who dies without agnatic heirs.
عصبہ (Asabah): Residuary heirs who inherit the remainder after the Quranic shares are distributed; in this context, the son is the primary asabah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Nothing
B) One-sixth
C) One-half
D) The entire wala'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi position on wala' inheritance is that the patron's father receives one-sixth and the son receives the remainder, based on the companion Shuraih's ruling.

Dispute on Wala' Inheritance: Grandfather vs Brother
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wala' inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on wala' inheritance when the patron's grandfather and brother both survive, explain the evidence for each view, and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between them.

Core Idea
Lineage to the grandfather overrides the brother's claim in wala', but the Sahibayn demand equal shares based on qiyas.

This passage presents a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the distribution of wala' when a freed slave dies leaving only the patron's grandfather and brother. The core question is whether the grandfather exclusively inherits the wala' or whether they share equally. Understanding this ikhtilaf illuminates how different usul principles—lineage tracing versus general agnatic inheritance—produce divergent rulings even within the same school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heirs and the issue
The deceased is a freed slave (mawla) who leaves the patron's grandfather (jadd) and brother (akh). No other agnatic heirs exist. The dispute is about which of these two inherits the wala' (the right to inherit the mawla's estate).
Wala' is a legal right of inheritance that passes to the patron's agnatic heirs.
2
State the two Hanafi positions
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the entire wala' goes to the grandfather. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that it is divided equally between grandfather and brother.
Each position is supported by a different hadith.
3
Examine the proofs (dalil)
Abu Hanifa cites a hadith from al-Zuhri: the wala' belongs to the grandfather because the patron is traced to him (lineage). The Sahibayn cite a hadith from Ata: the wala' is divided equally between them.
The difference in evidence reflects different usul principles.
4
Understand the underlying usul principle
Abu Hanifa's principle: lineage tracing has priority; the patron is connected to the grandfather but not to the brother, so the wala' follows the closer agnatic connection. The Sahibayn apply qiyas to general inheritance rules where grandfather and brother are both agnates and share equally in the absence of a closer heir.
This shows how a difference in usul leads to a difference in furu'.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, if the grandfather is present, according to Abu Hanifa he takes the entire wala', while according to the Sahibayn they split equally. This affects the actual inheritance amount each receives.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is generally Abu Hanifa's view, though some later fatwas follow the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان ترک جد مولاه واخا مولاه فالمال للجد عند ابي حنيفة وقال ابو يوسف و محمد هو بينهما عن الزهري في رجل ترث جده واخاه قال الولاء للجد لانه ينسب الى الجد ولا ينسب الى الاخ عن عطاء فی رجل مات وترك مولى له وجده واخاه لمن ولاء مولاه ؟ قال عطاء الولاء بينهما نصفين رحمهما الله تعالى هو بينهما
If he (the freed slave) leaves the grandfather of the patron and the brother of the patron, then the wealth is for the grandfather according to Abu Hanifa, and Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said it is between them. From al-Zuhri concerning a man whose grandfather and brother inherit: he said the wala' is for the grandfather because he is traced to the grandfather and not traced to the brother. From Ata concerning a man who died and left his freed slave, his grandfather, and his brother: To whom does the wala' of his freed slave belong? Ata said: The wala' is between them half and half. May Allah have mercy on them both, it is between them.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the distribution of wala' when a freed slave dies leaving only the patron's grandfather and brother. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the grandfather exclusively inherits the wala'. His reasoning is based on the principle of lineage: the patron is traced to the grandfather (as the father's father) but not to the brother, so the wala' follows the closer agnatic connection. The evidence is the statement of al-Zuhri, who explicitly rules that the wala' goes to the grandfather because the patron is traced to him. In contrast, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that the grandfather and brother share the wala' equally, half each. They rely on the statement of Ata, who divides the wala' between them. The Sahibayn's general principle is that the grandfather and brother are both agnatic heirs and thus share equally in the absence of closer heirs. The phrase 'رحمهما الله تعالى هو بينهما' (May Allah have mercy on them both, it is between them) emphasizes the Sahibayn's position. This ikhtilaf shows how differences in usul (principle of lineage vs. general agnatic inheritance) lead to differing furu' (branch rulings).

Key Terms
جد (Jadd): The paternal grandfather of the patron (the master who freed the slave).
اخ (Akh): The full or consanguine brother of the patron.
ولاء (Wala'): The right of inheritance a patron has over the estate of a freed slave who dies without agnatic heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what happens to the wala' of a freed slave when the patron's grandfather and brother both survive?
A) The grandfather takes all
B) The brother takes all
C) They share equally
D) The wala' is invalid

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on wala' shows that lineage tracing (to grandfather) can override general agnatic sharing, with Abu Hanifa favoring grandfather exclusively while the Sahibayn advocate equal division.

Prohibition of Selling or Gifting Wala' and Dispute over Distribution
States ruling on wala' sale/gift and discusses intra-Hanafi dispute on distribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling or gifting wala', explain the Hanafi positions on its distribution between grandfather and brother, and apply the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
Wala' cannot be sold or gifted; its inheritance distribution among heirs is debated within the Hanafi school.

This passage establishes the clear prohibition of selling or gifting wala' (clientage) based on a hadith from Ibn 'Umar. It then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding how wala' is distributed when the freed slave dies leaving a grandfather and a brother. While Imam Abu Hanifa gives all wala' to the grandfather, the two disciples (Sahibayn) divide it equally. The author of the Sharh adopts the Sahibayn's view, supported by an athar from 'Ata. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying inheritance rules related to manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling and its evidence
Locate the statement that wala' is not sold or gifted. Identify the hadith cited (from Ibn 'Umar) and note the verb 'nahā' (forbade) which establishes the ruling as haram.
The prohibition applies to the right of wala' itself, not to the underlying inheritance.
2
Understand the concept of wala' and its inheritance right
Define wala' as the relationship of clientage from manumission. Recognize that wala' entitles the manumitter and his male heirs to inherit from the freed slave if the latter dies without heirs.
Wala' is a right, not a physical object, hence its transfer is restricted.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on distribution
Contrast the two positions: Abu Hanifa excludes the brother entirely in the presence of the grandfather; Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) split the wala' equally. Identify the evidence for each: Abu Hanifa uses qiyas (grandfather like father), Sahibayn rely on an athar from 'Ata.
The dispute is about the order of inheritance among asabah, not about the prohibition.
4
Determine the mufta bihi view
Note that the author of Al-Sharh al-Thamiri adopts the Sahibayn's view, considering the athar strong. This is the operative position for fatwa in this context.
The tarjih is based on a transmitted precedent (athar) over analogical reasoning.

The author of the Sharh follows the Sahibayn's view. The prohibition of selling or gifting wala' is agreed upon, and the hadith is clear. The dispute is only about distribution among heirs, not about the core prohibition.

Order of Inheritance for Dhawi al-Arham in Hanafi Law
Lists the categories of uterine relatives in order of inheritance priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recite the Hanafi order of inheritance for dhawī al-arḥām and apply it to determine which uterine relative takes precedence when multiple exist.

Core Idea
Uterine relatives inherit in a fixed order based on proximity to the deceased, not by gender or closeness of blood.

We have learned about Quranic heirs and asabah. But what about relatives like grandchildren through a daughter, or maternal uncles? The Hanafi school includes them under the category of dhawī al-arḥām (uterine relatives) who inherit only when no asabah or Quranic heirs exist. This passage provides a numbered list of these relatives in order of priority, from the most proximate to the most distant. Understanding this order is essential for solving inheritance cases where the deceased leaves no closer heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify who are dhawī al-arḥām
Dhawī al-arḥām are all blood relatives who are not entitled to a fixed share (fard) and are not asabah (agnatic heirs). In Hanafi law, they inherit after these categories are exhausted.
Examples: daughter's children, sister's children, maternal uncles/aunts.
2
Memorize the order from the list
The text numbers the categories from 1 to 10. Start with the most proximate: (1) daughter's children, (2) sister's children, (3) brother's daughter, (4) paternal uncle's daughter, (5) maternal uncle, (6) maternal aunt, (7) maternal grandfather, (8) consanguine paternal uncle, (9) paternal aunt, (10) consanguine brother's children.
Each entry also shows the relationship to the deceased (e.g., 'offspring of the deceased' for #1).
3
Apply the principle of proximity
The order is based on how close the relative is to the deceased in lineage. For example, daughter's children are closer than sister's children because daughter's children are directly descended from the deceased, while sister's children are two steps away. In practice, when multiple dhawī al-arḥām exist, only the one with the highest priority inherits; the rest are excluded.
This is analogous to the rule for asabah: closer in degree excludes more distant.

These relatives inherit only when there are no 'asabah (agnatic heirs) or Quranic fixed-share heirs. 6 min
The Order of Dhawi al-Arham in Hanafi Faraid
Listing of distant relatives in priority order for inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten classes of Dhawi al-Arham in their correct priority order and explain the principle of proximity that governs this order.

Core Idea
Dhawi al-Arham inherit in order of closeness: closer kin before distant.

This lesson presents the definitive Hanafi list of Dhawi al-Arham (distant relatives) arranged by priority. When no primary Quranic heirs (ashab al-fara'id) or residuary heirs (asabah) exist, these relatives inherit according to this order. The list is based on the principle of proximity: the closer the relation to the deceased, the higher the priority. Memorizing this order is essential for correctly distributing inheritance in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of Dhawi al-Arham
Learn that Dhawi al-Arham are relatives who are neither Quranic heirs nor residuaries. They inherit only when no such primary heirs exist.
In Hanafi faraid, they form a distinct class with a fixed priority list.
2
Memorize the ten classes in order
Go through each numbered class from 1 to 10, noting the relationship and how they are connected to the deceased (e.g., daughter's children are offspring of the deceased).
The list uses Urdu terms; learn the corresponding Arabic terms as well.
3
Identify the grouping principle
Notice that classes are grouped by generational distance: first the offspring of the deceased, then offspring of parents, then offspring of grandparents, etc.
This reflects the principle that nearer blood relations have higher priority.
4
Apply the order to a scenario
Given a set of Dhawi al-Arham, determine which one inherits first by comparing their class numbers.
Only one class inherits at a time; if one class exists, all lower classes are excluded.
5
Recognize cross-madhhab differences
Note that the Shafi'i and other schools have different rules for Dhawi al-Arham; in Hanafi, this list is operative.
This list is specific to the Hanafi school (zahir al-riwayah).

Chapter of Dhawi al-Arham. Dhawi al-Arham at a glance. These people will inherit in order of number. Serial number, Arabic term, Urdu translation, relationship to deceased. (1) Daughter's children: grandson, granddaughter – offspring of deceased. (2) Sister's children: nephew, niece – offspring of father's offspring. (3) Brother's daughter: brother's daughter – offspring of father's offspring. (4) Uncle's daughter: paternal cousin sister – offspring of father's father. (5) Maternal uncle: maternal uncle – offspring of mother's father. (6) Maternal aunt: maternal aunt – offspring of mother's father. (7) Maternal grandfather: maternal grandfather – mother's father. (8) Consanguine paternal uncle: step-paternal uncle – offspring of father's mother. (9) Paternal aunt: paternal aunt – offspring of father. (10) Consanguine brother's children: step-nephew, step-niece – offspring of mother.

Commentary
This passage is a list of Dhawi al-Arham (distant relatives) in the order of their priority to inherit when there are no primary heirs (Ashāb al-Furūḍ) or residuary heirs ('Aṣabāt). In the Hanafi school, Dhawi al-Arham are arranged in classes: first, the offspring of the deceased (e.g., daughter's children); then the offspring of the deceased's parents (e.g., sister's children, brother's daughter); then the offspring of the deceased's grandparents (e.g., uncle's daughter, maternal uncle); and so on. The closer in relation to the deceased takes precedence.

Key Terms
ذوی الارحام (dhawi al-arham): Distant relatives who are neither Quranic heirs (ashab al-fara'id) nor residuary heirs (asabah), but inherit in their absence according to a specific order.
میت کی نسبت (mayyit ki nisbat): The relationship of the heir to the deceased.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school arranges Dhawi al-Arham into ten priority classes based on proximity to the deceased; higher classes exclude lower ones.

Base Number for Two Halves or Half and Remainder
Rule and example for base number 2

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the base number (asl al-mas'alah) and distribute shares in inheritance cases where there are two halves or a half and the remainder.

Core Idea
When heirs take half each, the base is simply 2.

In inheritance calculations, the base number is the foundation for distributing shares. This lesson covers a fundamental rule: when the shares are two halves (each heir gets exactly 1/2) or when one heir gets half and the other gets the other half as a remainder, the base number is always 2. We will work through a classic example: a woman leaving a husband and a full sister.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heirs and their shares
List all surviving heirs and determine their Quranic shares or asabah status. In the example: husband (1/2 as fard) and full sister (1/2 as fard).
Confirm there is no child or other heir altering the shares.
2
Check the share pattern
If both shares are exactly half, or one is half and the other is the remainder (which is also half), the rule applies.
If shares are not halves, this rule does not apply.
3
Set the base number
Since the common denominator of two halves is 2, the base number (asl al-mas'alah) is set to 2.
Each 1/2 becomes 1 out of 2.
4
Assign integer shares
Multiply each faction by the base: husband gets 1, sister gets 1. Total shares = 2, matching the base.
No tashih (correction) or awl/radd needed.
5
Verify distribution
Ensure the sum of shares equals the base number. Here 1+1=2, so the estate is fully distributed.
The base 2 works perfectly.

Source Text

Chapter of Calculation of Inheritances. 3253: If there is in the case a half and a half, or a half and the remainder, then its base (asl) is from two. Chapter of Calculation of Inheritances. Necessary note: It has been stated previously that in old calculation, points (decimals) could not be measured, so they would multiply the base case to obtain an integer, then divide among the heirs. Commentary
This passage from the chapter on inheritance calculation (hisab al-fara'id) provides a fundamental rule: when the shares are two halves (e.g., two heirs each entitled to half) or a half and the remainder (i.e., one gets half and the other gets the rest which is also effectively half), the base number (asl al-mas'alah) is 2. The rule is illustrated with an example: a woman dies leaving her husband and her full sister. The husband's share is 1/2 (as a fard heir when there is no child), and the sister's share is also 1/2 (as a fard heir in the absence of father or male sibling). Since both shares have denominator 2, the base number is 2, and each gets 1 share out of 2. The passage also includes a note contrasting traditional calculation (which avoided fractions by multiplying the base to get integers) with modern calculator methods using decimals from 100. The table of fractions shows the conversion of common Quranic shares into percentages, useful for the new method.

اصل مسئلہ (asl al-mas'alah): The base number from which the shares of the heirs are derived, typically the least common denominator of the fractional shares.

Rules for Base Number in Faraid: Halves and Thirds
Rules for base number when halves or thirds appear

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the base number (asl al-masalah) for faraid cases involving halves and thirds, and apply both traditional and calculator-based distribution.

Core Idea
When shares are halves or thirds, the base number is the denominator that sums the shares to one whole.

In inheritance calculation (faraid), the base number (asl al-masalah) is the least common denominator of the fractional shares. This lesson covers two foundational rules: when two halves or a half plus residue are present, the base is 2; when a third (or two-thirds) plus residue is present, the base is 3. We'll also see how to use a calculator based on 100 for modern convenience.

Guided Path
1
Identify share types
Look at the heirs and their prescribed shares. Determine if the case involves two halves, or a half and a residue, or a third/two-thirds and a residue.
Residue means the remaining estate after prescribed shares, taken by asabah.
2
Apply Rule 1 for halves
If the total fractional shares sum to 1 using halves (e.g., two halves, or half + residue), set the base number (asl al-masalah) to 2. Each share of half corresponds to 1 part, residue also 1 part.
Example: Husband (1/2) + Sister (1/2) = 1, base=2.
3
Apply Rule 2 for thirds
If the case involves a third and a residue (e.g., mother 1/3 + uncle 2/3 as residue), or two-thirds and a residue, set the base number to 3.
Example: Mother (1/3) + Uncle (residue 2/3) = 1, base=3.
4
Distribute using traditional method
Multiply each share fraction by the base number to get whole parts, then assign to heirs. For half+residue: half=1, residue=1. For third+residue: third=1, residue=2.
The total parts equal the base number.
5
Convert to calculator method
Set the total estate to 100. Multiply 100 by each share fraction to get the percentage share. For halves: 50, 50. For thirds: 33.33, 66.66.
This method avoids fractions but preserves the same proportions.

Source Text

Chapter on Calculation of Inheritances

Necessary note: It has been mentioned before that in old calculation, points could not be used; thus in the original problem, multiplication was done to obtain a whole number, then distributed among heirs. But nowadays, the calculator always calculates fractions, which in English are called points and in Urdu 'ashariyya'. Therefore, in the calculation of inheritances, calculator calculation will be given. And the old calculation is also given so that both calculations are easy to understand. The new calculation is always done with 100.

(Shares at a glance)
S.No. Arabic Share Urdu Share Fraction Decimal Percentage Calculation
(1) Half Half 1/2 50 = 100 ÷ 2
(2) Quarter Quarter 1/4 25 = 100 ÷ 4
(3) Eighth Eighth 1/8 12.5 = 100 ÷ 8
(4) Two-thirds Two-thirds 2/3 66.66 = 100 × 2 ÷ 3
(5) Third Third 33.33 = 100 ÷ 3
(6) Sixth Sixth 1/6 16.66 = 100 ÷ 6

[3253] (1) When in a case there are two halves, or a half and a residue, then the base of the case is from 2.
If two people each receive half, the base is 2. For example, a woman leaves a husband and her sister: the husband gets half, and the sister also gets half. The case proceeds from 2, and each is given half, i.e., one each.
The case is formed as:
Deceased 2
Husband 1
Sister 1
Calculator calculation is as follows:
Deceased 100
Husband 50
Sister 50

In this case, the husband gets half of 100, i.e., 50, and the sister also gets half of 100 according to her share, i.e., 50. The case of half and residue is as follows: For example, a woman leaves a husband: he is given half as his share, and what remains is given to the paternal uncle as an asabah (residuary heir). The case is formed as:
Deceased 2
Husband 1
Paternal uncle 1
Calculator calculation is as follows:
Deceased 100
Husband 50
Paternal uncle 50

[3254] (2) And if in the case there is a third and a residue, or two-thirds and a residue, then the base is from 3.
For example, a woman leaves a mother and a [paternal] uncle: the mother gets one-third as her prescribed share, and the remaining two-thirds go to the uncle as an asabah. In this situation, the case proceeds from 3. The case is formed as:
3
Mother 1
Uncle 2
Calculator calculation is as follows:
Deceased 100
Mother 33.33
Uncle 66.66

In this case, the mother is given one-third as her share, which out of 100 is 33.33, and the remaining two-thirds, i.e., 66.66, is given to the uncle as an asabah. The case of two-thirds and residue is as follows: For example, the deceased leaves two daughters: they get two-thirds, and the remaining one-third goes to the [paternal] uncle as an asabah.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the calculation of inheritances (faraid) with two fundamental rules for determining the base number (asl al-masalah) of an inheritance problem. The author first explains the modern method using a calculator based on 100 (percentage) alongside the traditional method. A table lists common fractional shares and their decimal/percentage equivalents. Then two rules are given:

Rule 3253: When the case contains two halves (i.e., two heirs each entitled to a half share) OR a half plus a residue (the remaining estate going to a residuary heir), the base number is 2. The examples illustrate: a husband and sister each take half (both fard heirs), so base 2; and a husband takes half (fard), and the residue goes to the paternal uncle (asabah), also base 2. In the calculator method, the total is set to 100, and each half becomes 50.

Rule 3254: When the case contains a third and a residue, or two-thirds and a residue, the base is 3. Example: mother takes one-third (fard), paternal uncle takes the remaining two-thirds as asabah, base 3. For two-thirds and residue, e.g., two daughters take two-thirds (fard), and the remaining third goes to a residuary heir. Calculator: one-third = 33.33, two-thirds = 66.66.

The rules are axiomatic: the base number is the least common denominator that sums the shares to 1. These rulings are part of the standard Hanafi faraid methodology (Zahir al-Riwayah). No intra-madhhab disputes are mentioned here.

Key Terms
اصل مسئلہ (asl al-masalah): The base number from which the inheritance shares are calculated, representing the common denominator of the fractional shares.
نصف (nisf): Half (1/2), a prescribed share in inheritance.
مابقی (ma baqi): What remains of the estate after the prescribed shares have been distributed, usually given to residuary heirs (asabah).
عصبہ (asabah): Residuary heir(s) who take the remaining estate after the prescribed shares are allotted.
کلکولیٹر (kalkulator): Calculator; here referring to modern calculation using percentages (based on 100) instead of traditional fractional arithmetic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a faraid case where a husband is entitled to half and a paternal uncle takes the remaining residue, what is the base number (asl al-masalah)?
A) 2
B) 3
C) 4
D) 6

Key Takeaway
The base number for inheritance is the denominator that sums all prescribed shares to 1: use 2 for halves, 3 for thirds.

Half and Remainder Inheritance (Husband + Uncle)
Explaining the half and remainder faraid case with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the inheritance shares when a husband and a paternal uncle are the only heirs, applying the half share and asabah residue rule.

Core Idea
Husband takes half, uncle takes the remainder — no other heirs, no fractions.

This passage presents the simplest faraid scenario: a woman dies leaving only her husband and her paternal uncle. The husband's prescribed share is fixed at 1/2 (half) because the deceased has no children. The remaining half passes to the paternal uncle as a residuary heir (asabah). Let's walk through the calculation step by step.

Guided Path
1
List all surviving heirs
Identify every legal heir. In this case, only the husband and paternal uncle survive. No children, parents, or other relatives.
2
Assign the prescribed share (fard)
The husband gets 1/2 of the estate because the deceased has no child. This is based on Quran 4:12.
If a child existed, the husband would get 1/4.
3
Determine the residuary (asabah)
After the fard share, the remainder goes to the nearest male relative by paternal line. Here, the paternal uncle is the asabah.
Asabah inherit only after fard shares are taken.
4
Set the base number and allocate parts
The base number (asl al-mas'alah) is the LCM of the share denominators. Since husband's share denominator is 2, base is 2. Husband gets 1 part, uncle gets 1 part.
5
Convert to actual amounts
If the estate is, say, 100, each part = 100/2 = 50. So husband gets 50, uncle gets 50.

Commentary
This passage presents the faraid rule for when the deceased is a woman who leaves her husband and a paternal uncle. The husband's prescribed share is 1/2 (half) because the deceased has no child. The remaining half goes to the uncle as an asabah (residuary heir). The base number (asl al-mas'alah) is 2 because the husband's share denominator is 2. From base 2, the husband takes 1 part and the uncle takes the remaining 1 part. When converting to actual amounts (e.g., 100 as total estate), each part equals 50, so husband gets 50 and uncle gets 50. The calculation is straightforward: only two heirs, one with a prescribed share and one as residuary.

Key Terms
نصف (nisf): Half, the prescribed share of 1/2 for a husband when his wife dies without children.
عصبة (asabah): Residuary heir who inherits the remainder after prescribed shares, here the paternal uncle.
ما بقی (ma baqi): The remainder or surplus after all prescribed shares have been distributed.

Third and Two-Thirds Remainder Inheritance Cases
Explaining faraid cases with third or two-thirds and remainder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate inheritance shares for cases involving a prescribed share of 1/3 or 2/3 and identify the residuary heir's share.

Core Idea
When a prescribed share is 1/3 or 2/3, the base number is always 3, and the remainder goes to the asabah.

In this lesson, we examine two common faraid scenarios: a deceased leaving a mother and a paternal uncle, or two daughters and a paternal uncle. Both involve a prescribed share of 1/3 (mother) or 2/3 (daughters), with the remainder passing to the uncle as residuary heir (asabah). Because the prescribed share denominators are 3, the base number is straightforward: 3.

Guided Path
1
Identify all surviving heirs
List all heirs from the problem. For example, mother and paternal uncle, or two daughters and paternal uncle.
In these cases, only two classes of heirs: a Quranic heir and a residuary heir.
2
Assign prescribed shares
Determine the fard share for the eligible heir. Mother gets 1/3 if no child. Two or more daughters get 2/3 collectively if no son.
The prescribed share denominator is 3 in both cases.
3
Identify the residuary heir
The remaining heir (paternal uncle) takes the remainder of the estate as asabah (residuary heir).
No other residuary heir is present.
4
Determine the base number (asl al-mas'alah)
Since the prescribed share denominator is 3, the base number is 3. No need to find LCM of different denominators.
If both shares had different denominators, the base would be the LCM; but here both are based on 3.
5
Allocate parts and calculate shares
From base 3, assign parts: in mother case, mother gets 1 part, uncle gets 2 parts. In daughters case, daughters collectively get 2 parts (each daughter 1 part if two), uncle gets 1 part. Then multiply by the actual estate amount.
For example, if estate = 300, each part = 100.

[3254(2)] And if there is a third and remainder, or two-thirds and remainder, then its base is from three.

Deceased: 3

Commentary
This passage presents two subcases where the share is 1/3 (third) or 2/3 (two-thirds) along with a residuary heir. The rule: when a prescribed share is a third or two-thirds, the base number (asl al-mas'alah) is 3 because these fractions have denominator 3. 

First example: A woman dies leaving her mother and paternal uncle. The mother's prescribed share is 1/3 (since the deceased has no child and no multiple siblings). The uncle takes the remaining 2/3 as asabah. Base is 3: mother gets 1 part, uncle gets 2 parts.

Second example: The deceased leaves two daughters. Their prescribed share is 2/3 (since two or more daughters get 2/3 collectively when there is no son). The remaining 1/3 goes to the paternal uncle as asabah. Again base 3: two daughters collectively 2 parts, uncle 1 part. Note: The two daughters share equally in their 2 parts if there are two, each gets 1 part. The text shows 'two daughters' as one heir category, so they take 2 parts together.

In both cases, the total of prescribed and residuary equals 1, so no awl occurs.

Key Terms
ثلث (thuluth): One-third, a prescribed share (fard) given to certain heirs like the mother under specific conditions.
ثلثان (thuluthani): Two-thirds, the prescribed share for two or more daughters when there is no son.
اصل المسئلة (asl al-mas'alah): The base number from which the shares are allocated; in these cases, 3.

Key Takeaway
When the prescribed share is 1/3 or 2/3, the base number is always 3, and the remainder goes to the asabah.

Quarter and Remainder: Wife and Paternal Grandfather
Faraid case: quarter and remainder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the estate shares when a wife and paternal grandfather survive, applying the rule that the wife receives 1/4 and the grandfather takes the remainder as a residuary heir.

Core Idea
Wife gets 1/4, grandfather takes all remaining 3/4 as asabah.

In faraid, when a deceased leaves only a wife and a paternal grandfather, the wife's prescribed share is one-quarter. The grandfather, being a residuary heir (asabah), inherits everything left after the prescribed shares. This lesson walks through the calculation using base number 4.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heirs
List the surviving heirs: wife and paternal grandfather. The grandfather is an asabah.
2
Assign the wife's prescribed share
The wife receives 1/4 of the estate because the deceased has no children (Quran 4:12).
3
Determine the remainder for the asabah
After the wife's 1/4, the remaining 3/4 goes to the paternal grandfather as a residuary heir.
No other prescribed heirs.
4
Set the base number
The wife's share denominator is 4, so the base number is 4.
Base number is the common denominator of all shares.
5
Allocate parts
Wife gets 1 part, grandfather gets 3 parts. Total 4 parts.
Shares sum to the base, so no awl or radd needed.

[3255(3)] And if there is a quarter and remainder, or a quarter and half, its base is from four.
For example, a wife and grandfather are left: the wife gets one-quarter, and the grandfather gets the remaining three-quarters as a residuary heir. Therefore, the problem is from four. The problem is constructed as follows:
Deceased: 4
Wife: 1
Grandfather: 3
Calculator calculation:
Deceased: 100
Wife: 25
Grandfather: 75

This is the case of a quarter and half. A woman leaves a husband, one daughter, and a paternal uncle. Since there is one daughter, she gets half. And due to the presence of children, the husband gets one-quarter. The remaining one-quarter goes to the uncle as a residuary. The problem is worked from four.
The problem is constructed as follows:
Deceased: 4
Husband: 1
One daughter: 2
Uncle: 1
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage presents two subcases where a quarter (1/4) is involved. The rule: when a share is 1/4, the base number is 4 because the denominator is 4.

First example: Deceased leaves a wife and a paternal grandfather (who is an asabah). The wife's prescribed share is 1/4 (since the deceased has no children). The grandfather takes the remaining 3/4 as asabah. Base is 4: wife 1 part, grandfather 3 parts.

Second example: Deceased is a woman who leaves a husband, one daughter, and a paternal uncle. The husband gets 1/4 because there is a child (the daughter). The daughter gets 1/2 (half) because she is a single daughter. The remaining 1/4 goes to the uncle as asabah. All shares have denominators that can be resolved to base 4: 1/2 = 2/4, 1/4 = 1/4, and the remainder is 1/4. So base 4 works: husband 1, daughter 2, uncle 1. Total 4 parts.

In both cases, the shares sum to the base, so no awl.

Key Terms
ربع (rub'): One-quarter, a prescribed share for a spouse under certain conditions (e.g., wife when no child).
عصبہ (asabah): Residuary heir, here the paternal grandfather.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When only a wife and a paternal grandfather survive, the wife takes 1/4 and the grandfather takes all remaining 3/4 as asabah.

Quarter and Half: Husband, Daughter, and Paternal Uncle
Faraid case: quarter and half

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate faraid shares when a husband, one daughter, and a paternal uncle survive, applying the rules: husband 1/4, daughter 1/2, uncle takes remainder as asabah.

Core Idea
Daughter gets 1/2, husband gets 1/4, uncle gets remaining 1/4 as asabah.

This case involves a quarter and a half. The daughter receives half of the estate, the husband receives one-quarter because there is a child, and the paternal uncle takes the remaining quarter as a residuary heir. The base number is 4.

Guided Path
1
List the heirs
Husband, one daughter, paternal uncle. The daughter is fard, husband is fard, uncle is asabah.
2
Assign the daughter's share
A single daughter gets 1/2 of the estate (Quran 4:11).
3
Assign the husband's share
The husband gets 1/4 because there is a child (Quran 4:12).
4
Calculate the remainder for the uncle
Total prescribed: 1/2 + 1/4 = 3/4. Remainder = 1/4, which goes to the uncle as asabah.
5
Set base number and allocate parts
Base number is 4. Daughter gets 2 parts (since 1/2 = 2/4), husband 1 part, uncle 1 part.
Shares sum to 4 parts, no awl or radd.
